portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Fascism_sidebar" title="Template:Fascism sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Fascism_sidebar" title="Template talk:Fascism sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Fascism_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Fascism</b> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Far-right" title="Far-right">far-right</a>, <a href="/wiki/Authoritarianism" title="Authoritarianism">authoritarian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ultranationalism" title="Ultranationalism">ultranationalist</a> political <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_ideology" title="Political ideology">ideology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Political_movement" title="Political movement">movement</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-m-w_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-m-w-3">[3]</a></sup> characterized by a <a href="/wiki/Dictator" title="Dictator">dictatorial</a> leader, centralized <a href="/wiki/Autocracy" title="Autocracy">autocracy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Militarism" title="Militarism">militarism</a>, forcible suppression of opposition, belief in a natural social hierarchy, subordination of <a href="/wiki/Individualism" title="Individualism">individual interests</a> for the perceived good of the nation and race, and strong regimentation of society and the economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-m-w_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-m-w-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fascism rose to prominence in early 20th-century Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeCompanion_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeCompanion-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInternational_Encyclopedia_of_Political_Science887–888Fascism_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInternational_Encyclopedia_of_Political_Science887–888Fascism-5">[5]</a></sup> The first fascist movements <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_Fascism" title="Italian Fascism">emerged in Italy</a> during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, before <a href="/wiki/Fascism_in_Europe" title="Fascism in Europe">spreading to other European countries</a>, most notably <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Germany</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeCompanion_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeCompanion-4">[4]</a></sup> Fascism also had adherents outside of Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEncyclopedia_Britannica_''Fascism''_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEncyclopedia_Britannica_''Fascism''-6">[6]</a></sup> Opposed to <a href="/wiki/Anarchism" title="Anarchism">anarchism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democracy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pluralism_(political_philosophy)" title="Pluralism (political philosophy)">pluralism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInternational_Encyclopedia_of_Political_Science889Fascism_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInternational_Encyclopedia_of_Political_Science889Fascism-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USHMM_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-USHMM-8">[8]</a></sup> fascism is placed on the far-right wing within the traditional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Left%E2%80%93right_spectrum" title="Left–right spectrum">left–right spectrum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeCompanion_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeCompanion-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USHMM_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-USHMM-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-University-Aristotle-Hartley-Wilhelm-Hawkesworth_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-University-Aristotle-Hartley-Wilhelm-Hawkesworth-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fascists saw <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Revolution" title="Revolution">revolution</a> that brought massive changes to the nature of war, society, the state, and technology. The advent of <a href="/wiki/Total_war" title="Total war">total war</a> and the mass mobilization of society erased the distinction between civilians and combatants. A military citizenship arose in which all citizens were involved with the military in some manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlamires2006140–141,_670Mann200465_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlamires2006140–141,_670Mann200465-10">[10]</a></sup> The war resulted in the rise of a powerful state capable of mobilizing millions of people to serve on the front lines and providing logistics to support them, as well as having unprecedented authority to intervene in the lives of citizens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlamires2006140–141,_670Mann200465_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlamires2006140–141,_670Mann200465-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fascism rejects assertions that violence is inherently bad and views <a href="/wiki/Imperialism" title="Imperialism">imperialism</a>, political violence and war as means to national rejuvenation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrčić2000120GriffinFeldman2004c185Spielvogel2012935Payne1995106_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrčić2000120GriffinFeldman2004c185Spielvogel2012935Payne1995106-11">[11]</a></sup> Fascists often advocate for the establishment of a <a href="/wiki/Totalitarianism" title="Totalitarianism">totalitarian</a> <a href="/wiki/One-party_state" title="One-party state">one-party state</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriffin20131–6_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriffin20131–6-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMussolini200240_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMussolini200240-13">[13]</a></sup> and for a <a href="/wiki/Dirigisme" title="Dirigisme">dirigiste</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> economy, with the principal goal of achieving <a href="/wiki/Autarky" title="Autarky">autarky</a> (national economic self-sufficiency) through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protectionist" title="Protectionist">protectionist</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Economic_interventionist" title="Economic interventionist">economic interventionist</a> policies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlamires2006188–189_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlamires2006188–189-16">[16]</a></sup> Fascism's extreme authoritarianism and nationalism often manifests as belief in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Racial_purity" title="Racial purity">racial purity</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Master_race" title="Master race">master race</a>, usually blended with some variant of <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racism</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bigotry" title="Bigotry">bigotry</a> against a demonized "<a href="/wiki/Other_(philosophy)" title="Other (philosophy)">Other</a>", such as <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a>. These ideas have motivated fascist regimes to commit <a href="/wiki/Genocide" title="Genocide">genocides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Massacre" title="Massacre">massacres</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forced_sterilizations" title="Forced sterilizations">forced sterilizations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mass_killing" title="Mass killing">mass killings</a>, and forced <a href="/wiki/Deportation" title="Deportation">deportations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKallis2011Paxton1998Lancaster2011_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKallis2011Paxton1998Lancaster2011-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> in 1945, few parties have openly described themselves as fascist; the term is more often used <a href="/wiki/Fascist_(insult)" title="Fascist (insult)">pejoratively</a> by political opponents. The descriptions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neo-fascist" title="Neo-fascist">neo-fascist</a> or <i>post-fascist</i> are sometimes employed to describe contemporary parties with ideologies similar to, or rooted in, 20th-century fascist movements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeCompanion_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeCompanion-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEnciclopedia_Italiana_''Neofascismo''_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEnciclopedia_Italiana_''Neofascismo''-18">[18]</a></sup> Some opposition groups have adopted the label anti-fascist or <a href="/wiki/Anti-fascism" title="Anti-fascism">antifa</a> to signify their stance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The Italian term <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">fascismo</i></span> is derived from <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">fascio</i></span>, meaning 'bundle of sticks', ultimately from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a> word <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Fasces" title="Fasces">fasces</a></i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-m-w_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-m-w-3">[3]</a></sup> This was the name given to political organizations in Italy known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fasci" title="Fasci">fasci</a>, groups similar to <a href="/wiki/Guild" title="Guild">guilds</a> or <a href="/wiki/Syndicate" title="Syndicate">syndicates</a>. According to Italian fascist dictator <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a>'s own account, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fasci_d%27Azione_Rivoluzionaria" title="Fasci d'Azione Rivoluzionaria">Fasces of Revolutionary Action</a> were founded in Italy in 1915.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMussolini2006227_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMussolini2006227-20">[20]</a></sup> In 1919, Mussolini founded the <a href="/wiki/Fasci_Italiani_di_Combattimento" title="Fasci Italiani di Combattimento">Italian Fasces of Combat</a> in Milan, which became the <a href="/wiki/National_Fascist_Party" title="National Fascist Party">National Fascist Party</a> two years later. The Fascists came to associate the term with the ancient Roman fasces or <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fascio_littorio" title="Fascio littorio">fascio littorio</a></i></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFalasca-Zamponi200095_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFalasca-Zamponi200095-21">[21]</a></sup> a bundle of rods tied around an axe,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Roman" title="Ancient Roman">ancient Roman</a> symbol of the authority of the civic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Magistrates" title="Roman Magistrates">magistrate</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> carried by his <a href="/wiki/Lictor" title="Lictor">lictors</a>, which could be used for <a href="/wiki/Corporal_punishment" title="Corporal punishment">corporal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Capital_punishment" title="Capital punishment">capital punishment</a> at his command.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWebstersPayne1995_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWebstersPayne1995-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (October 2021)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The symbolism of the fasces suggested strength through unity: a single rod is easily broken, while the bundle is difficult to break.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoordan1995_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoordan1995-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (October 2021)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Similar symbols were developed by different fascist movements: for example, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Falange" title="Falange">Falange</a> symbol is five arrows joined by a yoke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEParkins2002_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEParkins2002-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (October 2021)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">Definitions</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Definitions_of_fascism" title="Definitions of fascism">Definitions of fascism</a></div>
<p>Historians, political scientists, and other scholars have long debated the exact nature of fascism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregor2009_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregor2009-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (October 2021)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/Ian_Kershaw" title="Ian Kershaw">Ian Kershaw</a> once wrote that "trying to define 'fascism' is like trying to nail jelly to the wall."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw2016228_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw2016228-28">[28]</a></sup> Each different group described as fascist has at least some unique elements, and many definitions of fascism have been criticized as either too broad or too narrow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayne19807Griffiths2000_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayne19807Griffiths2000-29">[29]</a></sup> According to many scholars, fascism—especially once in power—has historically attacked communism, conservatism, and parliamentary liberalism, attracting support primarily from the far-right.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-30">[30]</a></sup> Frequently cited as a standard definition by notable <a href="/wiki/Scholar" title="Scholar">scholars</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> such as <a href="/wiki/Roger_Griffin" title="Roger Griffin">Roger Griffin</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Randall_Schweller" title="Randall Schweller">Randall Schweller</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bo_Rothstein" title="Bo Rothstein">Bo Rothstein</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Federico_Finchelstein" title="Federico Finchelstein">Federico Finchelstein</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Russian_Fascism:_Traditions,_Tendencies,_Movements" title="Russian Fascism: Traditions, Tendencies, Movements">Stephen D. Shenfield</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> is that of historian <a href="/wiki/Stanley_G._Payne" title="Stanley G. Payne">Stanley G. Payne</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayne19807_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayne19807-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>Payne's definition of fascism focuses on three concepts:
</p>
<ol><li>"Fascist negations" – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-liberalism" title="Anti-liberalism">anti-liberalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anti-communism" title="Anti-communism">anti-communism</a>, and anti-<a href="/wiki/Conservatism" title="Conservatism">conservatism</a>.</li>
<li>"Fascist goals" – the creation of a nationalist <a href="/wiki/Dictatorship" title="Dictatorship">dictatorship</a> to regulate economic structure and to transform social relations within a modern, self-determined culture, and the expansion of the nation into an empire.</li>
<li>"Fascist style" – a political aesthetic of romantic symbolism, mass mobilization, a positive view of violence, and promotion of masculinity, youth, and charismatic authoritarian leadership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERamswell20179GriffinFeldman2004a258Kallis2003b84–85Renton199921_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERamswell20179GriffinFeldman2004a258Kallis2003b84–85Renton199921-38">[38]</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>In his book <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/How_Fascism_Works:_The_Politics_of_Us_and_Them" title="How Fascism Works: The Politics of Us and Them">How Fascism Works: The Politics of Us and Them</a></i>, Professor <a href="/wiki/Jason_Stanley" title="Jason Stanley">Jason Stanley</a> defined fascism as "a cult of the leader who promises national restoration in the face of humiliation brought on by supposed communists, Marxists and minorities and immigrants who are supposedly posing a threat to the character and the history of a nation." and that "The leader proposes that only he can solve it and all of his political opponents are enemies or traitors." Stanley says recent global events as of 2020<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Fascism&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, including the <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic" title="COVID-19 pandemic">pandemic</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2020%E2%80%932021_United_States_racial_unrest" title="2020–2021 United States racial unrest">protests</a>, have substantiated his concern about how fascist rhetoric is showing up in politics and policies around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/John_Lukacs" title="John Lukacs">John Lukacs</a> argues that there is no such thing as generic fascism. He claims that <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">communism</a> are essentially manifestations of <a href="/wiki/Populism" title="Populism">populism</a> and that states such as Nazi Germany and <a href="/wiki/Fascist_Italy_(1922%E2%80%931943)" title="Fascist Italy (1922–1943)">Fascist Italy</a> are more different than similar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELukacs1998118_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELukacs1998118-40">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Roger_Griffin" title="Roger Griffin">Roger Griffin</a> describes fascism as "a genus of political ideology whose mythic core in its various permutations is a <a href="/wiki/Palingenesis" title="Palingenesis">palingenetic</a> form of populist <a href="/wiki/Ultranationalism" title="Ultranationalism">ultranationalism</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriffin199126_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriffin199126-41">[41]</a></sup> Griffin describes the ideology as having three core components: "(i) the rebirth myth, (ii) populist ultra-nationalism, and (iii) the myth of decadence."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriffin1991201_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriffin1991201-42">[42]</a></sup> In Griffin's view, fascism is "a genuinely revolutionary, trans-class form of anti-liberal, and in the last analysis, anti-conservative nationalism" built on a complex range of theoretical and cultural influences. He distinguishes an inter-war period in which it manifested itself in elite-led but populist "armed party" politics opposing socialism and liberalism, and promising radical politics to rescue the nation from decadence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriffin2003_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriffin2003-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (October 2021)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Kershaw argues that the difference between fascism and other forms of right-wing authoritarianism in the <a href="/wiki/Interwar_period" title="Interwar period">Interwar period</a> is that the latter generally aimed "to conserve the existing social order", whereas fascism was "revolutionary", seeking to change society and obtain "total commitment" from the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw2016228-9_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw2016228-9-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <i>Against the Fascist Creep</i>, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Reid_Ross" title="Alexander Reid Ross">Alexander Reid Ross</a> writes regarding Griffin's view: "Following the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> and shifts in fascist organizing techniques, a number of scholars have moved toward the minimalist 'new consensus' refined by Roger Griffin: 'the mythic core' of fascism is 'a populist form of <a href="/wiki/Palingenetic_ultranationalism" title="Palingenetic ultranationalism">palingenetic ultranationalism</a>.' That means that fascism is an ideology that draws on old, ancient, and even arcane myths of racial, cultural, ethnic, and national origins to develop a plan for the 'new man.<span style="padding-right:.15em;">'</span>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoss20175_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoss20175-45">[45]</a></sup> Griffin himself explored this 'mythic' or 'eliminable' core of fascism with his concept of <i>post-fascism</i> to explore the continuation of Nazism in the modern era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriffin2008Chapter_8:_Fascism’s_New_Faces_(and_New_Facelessness)_in_the_'Post-Fascist'_Epoch_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriffin2008Chapter_8:_Fascism’s_New_Faces_(and_New_Facelessness)_in_the_'Post-Fascist'_Epoch-46">[46]</a></sup> Additionally, other historians have applied this minimalist core to explore <i>proto-fascist</i> movements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoel_Reyes2019_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoel_Reyes2019-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cas_Mudde" title="Cas Mudde">Cas Mudde</a> and Cristóbal Rovira Kaltwasser argue that although fascism "flirted with populism ... in an attempt to generate mass support", it is better seen as an elitist ideology. They cite in particular its exaltation of the Leader, the race, and the state, rather than the people. They see populism as a "thin-centered ideology" with a "restricted morphology" that necessarily becomes attached to "thick-centered" ideologies such as fascism, liberalism, or socialism. Thus populism can be found as an aspect of many specific ideologies, without necessarily being a defining characteristic of those ideologies. They refer to the combination of populism, authoritarianism and ultranationalism as "a marriage of convenience".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeKaltwasser20176,_33–34_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeKaltwasser20176,_33–34-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Robert_Paxton" title="Robert Paxton">Robert Paxton</a> says: "[fascism is] a form of political behavior marked by obsessive preoccupation with community decline, humiliation, or victimhood and by compensatory cults of unity, energy, and purity, in which a mass-based party of committed nationalist militants, working in uneasy but effective collaboration with traditional elites, abandons democratic liberties and pursues with redemptive violence and without ethical or legal restraints goals of internal cleansing and external expansion."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaxton2004216_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaxton2004216-50">[50]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Roger_Eatwell" title="Roger Eatwell">Roger Eatwell</a> defines fascism as "an ideology that strives to forge social rebirth based on a <a href="/wiki/Holism" title="Holism">holistic</a>-national radical <a href="/wiki/Third_Position" title="Third Position">Third Way</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatwell199624_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatwell199624-51">[51]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Walter_Laqueur" title="Walter Laqueur">Walter Laqueur</a> sees the core tenets of fascism as "self-evident: nationalism; <a href="/wiki/Social_Darwinism" title="Social Darwinism">social Darwinism</a>; racialism, the need for leadership, a new aristocracy, and obedience; and the negation of the ideals of the Enlightenment and the French Revolution."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaqueur199796_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaqueur199796-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Racism was a key feature of German fascism, for which the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holocaust" title="Holocaust">Holocaust</a> was a high priority. According to the historiography of <a href="/wiki/Genocide" title="Genocide">genocide</a>, "In dealing with the Holocaust, it is the consensus of historians that Nazi Germany targeted Jews as a race, not as a religious group."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeiss-WendtKriekenCave200873_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeiss-WendtKriekenCave200873-53">[53]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Umberto_Eco" title="Umberto Eco">Umberto Eco</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eco1995_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eco1995-54">[54]</a></sup> Kevin Passmore,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPassmore200231_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPassmore200231-55">[55]</a></sup> John Weiss,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeiss1967_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeiss1967-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (October 2021)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Ian Adams,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams1993_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams1993-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (October 2021)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and Moyra Grant<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrant200360–61_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrant200360–61-58">[58]</a></sup> stress <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racism</a> as a characteristic component of German fascism. Historian <a href="/wiki/Robert_Soucy" title="Robert Soucy">Robert Soucy</a> stated that "Hitler envisioned the ideal German society as a <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Volksgemeinschaft" title="Volksgemeinschaft">Volksgemeinschaft</a></i></span>, a racially unified and hierarchically organized body in which the interests of individuals would be strictly subordinate to those of the nation, or Volk."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEncyclopedia_Britannica_''Volksgemeinschaft''_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEncyclopedia_Britannica_''Volksgemeinschaft''-59">[59]</a></sup> Kershaw noted that common factors of fascism included "the ‘cleansing’ of all those deemed not to belong – foreigners, ethnic minorities, 'undesirables'" and belief in its own nation's superiority, even if it was not biological racism like in Nazism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw2016228-9_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw2016228-9-44">[44]</a></sup> Fascist philosophies vary by application, but remain distinct by one theoretical commonality: all traditionally fall into the far-right sector of any <a href="/wiki/Political_spectrum" title="Political spectrum">political spectrum</a>, catalyzed by afflicted class identities over conventional social inequities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeCompanion_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeCompanion-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Position_in_the_political_spectrum">Position in the political spectrum</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Francoist_demonstration_in_Salamanca.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2019" data-file-width="2683" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Francoist_demonstration_in_Salamanca.jpg/220px-Francoist_demonstration_in_Salamanca.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Francoist_demonstration_in_Salamanca.jpg/330px-Francoist_demonstration_in_Salamanca.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Francoist_demonstration_in_Salamanca.jpg/440px-Francoist_demonstration_in_Salamanca.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Francoist_demonstration_in_Salamanca.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pro-government demonstration in <a href="/wiki/Salamanca" title="Salamanca">Salamanca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francoist_Spain" title="Francoist Spain">Francoist Spain</a> in 1937. <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Franco" title="Francisco Franco">Francisco Franco</a> was labeled by foreign opinion the "last surviving fascist dictator".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Scholars place fascism on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Far_right" title="Far right">far-right</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Political_spectrum" title="Political spectrum">political spectrum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeCompanion_4-5"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeCompanion-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USHMM_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-USHMM-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-University-Aristotle-Hartley-Wilhelm-Hawkesworth_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-University-Aristotle-Hartley-Wilhelm-Hawkesworth-9">[9]</a></sup> Such scholarship focuses on its <a href="/wiki/Social_conservatism" title="Social conservatism">social conservatism</a> and its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Authoritarian" title="Authoritarian">authoritarian</a> means of opposing <a href="/wiki/Egalitarianism" title="Egalitarianism">egalitarianism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaviesLynch2002126–127Zafirovski2008137–138_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaviesLynch2002126–127Zafirovski2008137–138-61">[61]</a></sup> Roderick Stackelberg places fascism—including <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazism</a>, which he says is "a radical variant of fascism"—on the political right by explaining: "The more a person deems absolute equality among all people to be a desirable condition, the further left he or she will be on the ideological spectrum. The more a person considers inequality to be unavoidable or even desirable, the further to the right he or she will be."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStackelberg19994–6_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStackelberg19994–6-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fascism's origins are complex and include many seemingly contradictory viewpoints, ultimately centered on a mythos of national rebirth from decadence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriffin2003_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriffin2003-43">[43]</a></sup> Fascism was founded during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> by Italian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_syndicalists" title="National syndicalists">national syndicalists</a> who drew upon both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Left-wing" title="Left-wing">left-wing</a> organizational tactics and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right-wing" title="Right-wing">right-wing</a> political views.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregor2009191_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregor2009191-63">[63]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_Fascism" title="Italian Fascism">Italian Fascism</a> gravitated to the right in the early 1920s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri1994161BorsellaCaso200776_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri1994161BorsellaCaso200776-64">[64]</a></sup> A major element of fascist ideology that has been deemed to be far right is its stated goal to promote the right of a supposedly superior people to dominate, while purging society of supposedly inferior elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoshinsky2008156_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoshinsky2008156-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1920s, the Italian Fascists described their ideology as right-wing in the political program <i><a href="/wiki/The_Doctrine_of_Fascism" title="The Doctrine of Fascism">The Doctrine of Fascism</a></i>, stating: "We are free to believe that this is the century of authority, a century tending to the 'right,' a fascist century."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchnappSearsStampino200057Mussolini193526_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchnappSearsStampino200057Mussolini193526-66">[66]</a></sup> Mussolini stated that fascism's position on the political spectrum was not a serious issue for fascists: "fascism, sitting on the right, could also have sat on the mountain of the center. ... These words in any case do not have a fixed and unchanged meaning: they do have a variable subject to location, time and spirit. We don't give a damn about these empty terminologies and we despise those who are terrorized by these words."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>Major Italian groups politically on the right, especially rich landowners and big business, feared an uprising by groups on the left, such as sharecroppers and labour unions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaranskiWest200150–51_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaranskiWest200150–51-68">[68]</a></sup> They welcomed fascism and supported its violent suppression of opponents on the left.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEncyclopedia_Britannica_''The_fascist_era''_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEncyclopedia_Britannica_''The_fascist_era''-69">[69]</a></sup> The accommodation of the political right into the Italian Fascist movement in the early 1920s created internal factions within the movement. The "Fascist left" included <a href="/wiki/Michele_Bianchi" title="Michele Bianchi">Michele Bianchi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Bottai" title="Giuseppe Bottai">Giuseppe Bottai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Angelo_Oliviero_Olivetti" title="Angelo Oliviero Olivetti">Angelo Oliviero Olivetti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sergio_Panunzio" title="Sergio Panunzio">Sergio Panunzio</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Edmondo_Rossoni" title="Edmondo Rossoni">Edmondo Rossoni</a>, who were committed to advancing national syndicalism as a replacement for parliamentary liberalism in order to modernize the economy and advance the interests of workers and the common people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayne1995112_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayne1995112-70">[70]</a></sup> The "fascist right" included members of the paramilitary <a href="/wiki/Blackshirts" title="Blackshirts">Blackshirts</a> and former members of the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Nationalist_Association" title="Italian Nationalist Association">Italian Nationalist Association</a> (ANI).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayne1995112_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayne1995112-70">[70]</a></sup> The Blackshirts wanted to establish fascism as a complete dictatorship, while the former ANI members, including <a href="/wiki/Alfredo_Rocco" title="Alfredo Rocco">Alfredo Rocco</a>, sought to institute an authoritarian corporatist state to replace the liberal state in Italy while retaining the existing elites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayne1995112_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayne1995112-70">[70]</a></sup> Upon accommodating the political right, there arose a group of monarchist fascists who sought to use fascism to create an <a href="/wiki/Absolute_monarchy" title="Absolute monarchy">absolute monarchy</a> under King <a href="/wiki/Victor_Emmanuel_III_of_Italy" title="Victor Emmanuel III of Italy">Victor Emmanuel III of Italy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayne1995112_70-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayne1995112-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Fascist_regime_in_Italy" title="Fall of the Fascist regime in Italy">fall of the Fascist regime in Italy</a>, when King Victor Emmanuel III forced Mussolini to resign as head of government and placed him under arrest in 1943, Mussolini was rescued by German forces. While continuing to rely on Germany for support, Mussolini and the remaining loyal Fascists founded the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Social_Republic" title="Italian Social Republic">Italian Social Republic</a> with Mussolini as head of state. Mussolini sought to re-radicalize Italian Fascism, declaring that the fascist state had been overthrown because Italian fascism had been subverted by Italian conservatives and the bourgeoisie.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallBellamy2003133_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallBellamy2003133-71">[71]</a></sup> Then the new fascist government proposed the creation of workers' councils and profit-sharing in industry, although the German authorities, who effectively controlled northern Italy at this point, ignored these measures and did not seek to enforce them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallBellamy2003133_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallBellamy2003133-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>A number of post-World War II fascist movements described themselves as a <a href="/wiki/Third_Position" title="Third Position">Third Position</a> outside the traditional political spectrum. <a href="/wiki/Falange_Espa%C3%B1ola_de_las_JONS" title="Falange Española de las JONS">Falange Española de las JONS</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Antonio_Primo_de_Rivera" title="José Antonio Primo de Rivera">José Antonio Primo de Rivera</a> said: "[B]asically the Right stands for the maintenance of an economic structure, albeit an unjust one, while the Left stands for the attempt to subvert that economic structure, even though the subversion thereof would entail the destruction of much that was worthwhile."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeocleous199754_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeocleous199754-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fascist_as_a_pejorative"><i>Fascist</i> as a pejorative</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fascist_(insult)" title="Fascist (insult)">Fascist (insult)</a></div>
<p>The term <i>fascist</i> has been used as a <a href="/wiki/Pejorative" title="Pejorative">pejorative</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregor20054_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregor20054-73">[73]</a></sup> regarding varying movements across the far right of the political spectrum. <a href="/wiki/George_Orwell" title="George Orwell">George Orwell</a> noted in 1944 that the term had been used to denigrate diverse positions "in internal politics": while fascism is "a political and economic system" that was inconvenient to define, "<i>as used</i>, the word 'Fascism' is almost entirely meaningless. ... almost any English person would accept 'bully' as a synonym for 'Fascist,<span style="padding-right:.15em;">'</span>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOrwell2019_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOrwell2019-74">[74]</a></sup><small>(<a href="/wiki/Emphasis_(typography)" title="Emphasis (typography)">emphasis added</a>)</small>, and in 1946 wrote that "...'Fascism' has now no meaning except in so far as it signifies something not desirable."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOrwell1946_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOrwell1946-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite fascist movements' history of <a href="/wiki/Anti-communism" title="Anti-communism">anti-communism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Communist_state" title="Communist state">Communist states</a> have sometimes been referred to as <i>fascist</i>, typically as an insult. It has been applied to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marxist%E2%80%93Leninist" title="Marxist–Leninist">Marxist–Leninist</a> regimes in <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> under <a href="/wiki/Fidel_Castro" title="Fidel Castro">Fidel Castro</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> under <a href="/wiki/Ho_Chi_Minh" title="Ho Chi Minh">Ho Chi Minh</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriffinFeldman2004a231_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriffinFeldman2004a231-76">[76]</a></sup> Chinese Marxists used the term to denounce the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Sino-Soviet_split" title="Sino-Soviet split">Sino-Soviet split</a>, and the Soviets used the term to denounce Chinese Marxists<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuarantotto1976_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuarantotto1976-77">[77]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Social_democracy" title="Social democracy">social democracy</a>, coining a new term in <i><a href="/wiki/Social_fascism" title="Social fascism">social fascism</a></i>.
</p><p>In the United States, Herbert Matthews of <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> asked in 1946: "Should we now place Stalinist Russia in the same category as Hitlerite Germany? Should we say that she is Fascist?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthews1946_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthews1946-78">[78]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/J._Edgar_Hoover" title="J. Edgar Hoover">J. Edgar Hoover</a>, longtime <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FBI" title="FBI">FBI</a> director and ardent anti-communist, wrote extensively of <a href="/wiki/Red_fascism" title="Red fascism">red fascism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoover1947_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoover1947-79">[79]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ku_Klux_Klan" title="Ku Klux Klan">Ku Klux Klan</a> in the 1920s was sometimes called <i>fascist</i>. Historian Peter Amann states that, "Undeniably, the Klan had some traits in common with European fascism—chauvinism, racism, a mystique of violence, an affirmation of a certain kind of archaic traditionalism—yet their differences were fundamental ... [the KKK] never envisioned a change of political or economic system."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmann1986562_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmann1986562-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>Professor Richard Griffiths of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wales" title="University of Wales">University of Wales</a> wrote in 2005 that "fascism" is the "most misused, and over-used word, of our times."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriffiths2000_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriffiths2000-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (October 2021)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="How can a source from 2000 be used to support a statement from 2005? (October 2021)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> "Fascist" is sometimes applied to post-World War II organizations and ways of thinking that academics more commonly term <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neo-fascist" title="Neo-fascist">neo-fascist</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoolf198118_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoolf198118-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Fascism_and_ideology" title="Fascism and ideology">Fascism and ideology</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Background_and_19th-century_roots">Background and 19th-century roots</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Georges_Valois" title="Georges Valois">Georges Valois</a>, founder of the first non-Italian fascist party <a href="/wiki/Faisceau" title="Faisceau">Faisceau</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESternhell1976_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESternhell1976-83">[83]</a></sup> claimed the roots of fascism stemmed from the late 18th century <a href="/wiki/Jacobin_(politics)" title="Jacobin (politics)">Jacobin</a> movement, seeing in its totalitarian nature a foreshadowing of the fascist state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECamusLebourg201720_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECamusLebourg201720-84">[84]</a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/George_Mosse" title="George Mosse">George Mosse</a> similarly analyzed fascism as an inheritor of the <a href="/wiki/Mass_movement_(politics)" title="Mass movement (politics)">mass ideology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Civil_religion" title="Civil religion">civil religion</a> of the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>, as well as a result of the brutalization of societies in 1914–1918.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECamusLebourg201720_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECamusLebourg201720-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>Historians such as <a href="/wiki/Irene_Collins" title="Irene Collins">Irene Collins</a> and Howard C Payne see <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_III" title="Napoleon III">Napoleon III</a>, who ran a 'police state' and suppressed the media, as a forerunner of fascism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams201528_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams201528-85">[85]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/David_Thomson_(historian)" title="David Thomson (historian)">David Thomson</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomson1966293_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomson1966293-86">[86]</a></sup> the Italian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Risorgimento" title="Risorgimento">Risorgimento</a> of 1871 led to the 'nemesis of fascism'. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_L_Shirer" title="William L Shirer">William L Shirer</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer196097_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer196097-87">[87]</a></sup> sees a continuity from the views of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fichte" title="Fichte">Fichte</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hegel" title="Hegel">Hegel</a>, through <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Bismarck" title="Otto von Bismarck">Bismarck</a>, to Hitler; <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gerwarth" title="Robert Gerwarth">Robert Gerwarth</a> speaks of a 'direct line' from Bismarck to Hitler.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGerwarth2005166_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGerwarth2005166-88">[88]</a></sup> Julian Dierkes sees fascism as a 'particularly violent form of <a href="/wiki/Imperialism" title="Imperialism">imperialism</a>'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDierkes201054_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDierkes201054-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Fin_de_si.C3.A8cle_era_and_fusion_of_Maurrasism_with_Sorelianism_.281880.E2.80.931914.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Fin_de_siècle_era_and_fusion_of_Maurrasism_with_Sorelianism_(1880–1914)"><span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">Fin de siècle</i></span> era and fusion of Maurrasism with Sorelianism (1880–1914)</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/National_syndicalism" title="National syndicalism">National syndicalism</a></div>
<p>The historian <a href="/wiki/Zeev_Sternhell" title="Zeev Sternhell">Zeev Sternhell</a> has traced the ideological roots of fascism back to the 1880s and in particular to the <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr"><a href="/wiki/Fin_de_si%C3%A8cle" title="Fin de siècle">fin de siècle</a></i></span> theme of that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESternhell1998169Payne199523–24_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESternhell1998169Payne199523–24-90">[90]</a></sup> The theme was based on a revolt against <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">materialism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Positivism" title="Positivism">positivism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bourgeois" title="Bourgeois">bourgeois</a> society, and <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democracy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESternhell1998170_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESternhell1998170-91">[91]</a></sup> The <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">fin-de-siècle</i></span> generation supported <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emotionalism_(disorder)" title="Emotionalism (disorder)">emotionalism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irrationalism" title="Irrationalism">irrationalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Subjectivism" title="Subjectivism">subjectivism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vitalism" title="Vitalism">vitalism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayne199524_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayne199524-92">[92]</a></sup> They regarded civilization as being in crisis, requiring a massive and total solution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESternhell1998170_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESternhell1998170-91">[91]</a></sup> Their intellectual school considered the individual as only one part of the larger collectivity, which should not be viewed as a numerical sum of atomized individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESternhell1998170_91-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESternhell1998170-91">[91]</a></sup> They condemned the rationalistic, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liberal_individualism" title="Liberal individualism">liberal individualism</a> of society and the dissolution of social links in bourgeois society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESternhell1998170_91-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESternhell1998170-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">fin-de-siècle</i></span> outlook was influenced by various intellectual developments, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darwinian" title="Darwinian">Darwinian</a> biology, <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Gesamtkunstwerk" title="Gesamtkunstwerk">Gesamtkunstwerk</a></i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_de_Gobineau" title="Arthur de Gobineau">Arthur de Gobineau</a>'s racialism, <a href="/wiki/Gustave_Le_Bon" title="Gustave Le Bon">Gustave Le Bon</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a>, and the philosophies of <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Friedrich Nietzsche">Friedrich Nietzsche</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fyodor_Dostoyevsky" title="Fyodor Dostoyevsky">Fyodor Dostoyevsky</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Henri_Bergson" title="Henri Bergson">Henri Bergson</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESternhell1998171_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESternhell1998171-93">[93]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Social_Darwinism" title="Social Darwinism">Social Darwinism</a>, which gained widespread acceptance, made no distinction between physical and social life, and viewed the human condition as being an unceasing struggle to achieve the <a href="/wiki/Survival_of_the_fittest" title="Survival of the fittest">survival of the fittest</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESternhell1998171_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESternhell1998171-93">[93]</a></sup> It challenged positivism's claim of deliberate and rational choice as the determining behaviour of humans, with social Darwinism focusing on heredity, race, and environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESternhell1998171_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESternhell1998171-93">[93]</a></sup> Its emphasis on biogroup identity and the role of organic relations within societies fostered the legitimacy and appeal of nationalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayne199529_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayne199529-94">[94]</a></sup> New theories of social and political psychology also rejected the notion of human behaviour being governed by rational choice and instead claimed that emotion was more influential in political issues than reason.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESternhell1998171_93-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESternhell1998171-93">[93]</a></sup> Nietzsche's argument that "God is dead" coincided with his attack on the "herd mentality" of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democracy</a>, and modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collectivism_and_individualism" title="Collectivism and individualism">collectivism</a>, his concept of the <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/%C3%9Cbermensch" title="Übermensch">Übermensch</a></i></span>, and his advocacy of the <a href="/wiki/Will_to_power" title="Will to power">will to power</a> as a primordial instinct, were major influences upon many of the <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">fin-de-siècle</i></span> generation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayne199524–25_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayne199524–25-95">[95]</a></sup> Bergson's claim of the existence of an <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">élan vital</i></span>, or vital instinct, centred upon free choice and rejected the processes of materialism and determinism; this challenged Marxism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayne199525_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayne199525-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:241px;max-width:241px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:122px;max-width:122px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charles_Maurras_-_avant_1922.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="363" data-file-width="295" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Charles_Maurras_-_avant_1922.jpg/120px-Charles_Maurras_-_avant_1922.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Charles_Maurras_-_avant_1922.jpg/180px-Charles_Maurras_-_avant_1922.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Charles_Maurras_-_avant_1922.jpg/240px-Charles_Maurras_-_avant_1922.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Charles_Maurras" title="Charles Maurras">Charles Maurras</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:115px;max-width:115px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Georges_Sorel.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="744" data-file-width="567" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Georges_Sorel.jpg/113px-Georges_Sorel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Georges_Sorel.jpg/170px-Georges_Sorel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Georges_Sorel.jpg/226px-Georges_Sorel.jpg 2x" width="113"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Georges_Sorel" title="Georges Sorel">Georges Sorel</a></div></div></div></div></div>
<p>In his work <i>The Ruling Class</i> (1896), <a href="/wiki/Gaetano_Mosca" title="Gaetano Mosca">Gaetano Mosca</a> developed the theory that claims that in all societies an "organized minority" would dominate and rule over an "disorganized majority",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOuthwaite2006442Koon19856_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOuthwaite2006442Koon19856-97">[97]</a></sup> stating that there are only two classes in society, "the governing" (the organized minority) and "the governed" (the disorganized majority).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaforio200612_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaforio200612-98">[98]</a></sup> He claims that the organized nature of the organized minority makes it irresistible to any individual of the disorganized majority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaforio200612_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaforio200612-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_nationalist" title="French nationalist">French nationalist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reactionary" title="Reactionary">reactionary</a> monarchist <a href="/wiki/Charles_Maurras" title="Charles Maurras">Charles Maurras</a> influenced fascism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarroll199892_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarroll199892-99">[99]</a></sup> Maurras promoted what he called <a href="/wiki/Integral_nationalism" title="Integral nationalism">integral nationalism</a>, which called for the organic unity of a nation, and insisted that a powerful monarch was an ideal leader of a nation. Maurras distrusted what he considered the democratic mystification of the popular will that created an impersonal collective subject.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarroll199892_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarroll199892-99">[99]</a></sup> He claimed that a powerful monarch was a personified sovereign who could exercise authority to unite a nation's people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarroll199892_99-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarroll199892-99">[99]</a></sup> Maurras' integral nationalism was idealized by fascists, but modified into a modernized revolutionary form that was devoid of Maurras' monarchism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarroll199892_99-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarroll199892-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fascist_syndicalism">Fascist syndicalism</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fascist_syndicalism" title="Fascist syndicalism">Fascist syndicalism</a></div>
<p>French revolutionary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syndicalist" title="Syndicalist">syndicalist</a> <a href="/wiki/Georges_Sorel" title="Georges Sorel">Georges Sorel</a> promoted the legitimacy of <a href="/wiki/Political_violence" title="Political violence">political violence</a> in his work <i><a href="/wiki/Reflections_on_Violence" title="Reflections on Violence">Reflections on Violence</a></i> (1908) and other works in which he advocated radical syndicalist action to achieve a revolution to overthrow capitalism and the bourgeoisie through a <a href="/wiki/General_strike" title="General strike">general strike</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAntliff200775–81_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAntliff200775–81-100">[100]</a></sup> In <i>Reflections on Violence</i>, Sorel emphasized need for a revolutionary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_religion" title="Political religion">political religion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAntliff200781_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAntliff200781-101">[101]</a></sup> Also in his work <i>The Illusions of Progress</i>, Sorel denounced democracy as reactionary, saying "nothing is more aristocratic than democracy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAntliff200777_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAntliff200777-102">[102]</a></sup> By 1909, after the failure of a syndicalist general strike in France, Sorel and his supporters left the radical left and went to the radical right, where they sought to merge militant Catholicism and French patriotism with their views—advocating anti-republican Christian French patriots as ideal revolutionaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAntliff200782_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAntliff200782-103">[103]</a></sup> Initially, Sorel had officially been a <a href="/wiki/Revisionism_(Marxism)" title="Revisionism (Marxism)">revisionist</a> of Marxism, but by 1910 announced his abandonment of socialist literature and claimed in 1914, using an aphorism of <a href="/wiki/Benedetto_Croce" title="Benedetto Croce">Benedetto Croce</a> that "socialism is dead" because of the "decomposition of Marxism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri199478_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri199478-104">[104]</a></sup> Sorel became a supporter of reactionary Maurrassian nationalism beginning in 1909 that influenced his works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri199478_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri199478-104">[104]</a></sup> Maurras held interest in merging his nationalist ideals with Sorelian <a href="/wiki/Syndicalism" title="Syndicalism">syndicalism</a>, known as <a href="/wiki/Sorelianism" title="Sorelianism">Sorelianism</a>, as a means to confront democracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri199482_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri199482-105">[105]</a></sup> Maurras stated that "a socialism liberated from the democratic and cosmopolitan element fits nationalism well as a well made glove fits a beautiful hand."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolmes200060_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolmes200060-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EnricoCorradini.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="369" data-file-width="269" decoding="async" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/EnricoCorradini.jpg/170px-EnricoCorradini.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/EnricoCorradini.jpg/255px-EnricoCorradini.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/43/EnricoCorradini.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EnricoCorradini.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Enrico_Corradini" title="Enrico Corradini">Enrico Corradini</a></div></div></div>
<p>The fusion of Maurrassian nationalism and Sorelian syndicalism influenced radical Italian nationalist <a href="/wiki/Enrico_Corradini" title="Enrico Corradini">Enrico Corradini</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri1994163_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri1994163-107">[107]</a></sup> Corradini spoke of the need for a nationalist-syndicalist movement, led by elitist aristocrats and anti-democrats who shared a revolutionary syndicalist commitment to direct action and a willingness to fight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri1994163_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri1994163-107">[107]</a></sup> Corradini spoke of Italy as being a "proletarian nation" that needed to pursue imperialism in order to challenge the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plutocratic" title="Plutocratic">plutocratic</a>" French and British.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlinkhorn200612_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlinkhorn200612-108">[108]</a></sup> Corradini's views were part of a wider set of perceptions within the right-wing Italian Nationalist Association (ANI), which claimed that Italy's economic backwardness was caused by corruption in its political class, liberalism, and division caused by "ignoble socialism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlinkhorn200612_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlinkhorn200612-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>The ANI held ties and influence among <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservatives" title="Conservatives">conservatives</a>, Catholics, and the business community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlinkhorn200612–13_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlinkhorn200612–13-109">[109]</a></sup> Italian national syndicalists held a common set of principles: the rejection of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bourgeois" title="Bourgeois">bourgeois</a> values, democracy, liberalism, <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Internationalism_(politics)" title="Internationalism (politics)">internationalism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pacifism" title="Pacifism">pacifism</a>, and the promotion of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heroism" title="Heroism">heroism</a>, vitalism, and violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri199432_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri199432-110">[110]</a></sup> The ANI claimed that liberal democracy was no longer compatible with the modern world, and advocated a strong state and imperialism. They believed that humans are naturally predatory, and that nations are in a constant struggle in which only the strongest would survive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGentile20036_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGentile20036-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Manifesto_of_Futurism.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="365" data-file-width="556" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Manifesto_of_Futurism.jpg/220px-Manifesto_of_Futurism.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Manifesto_of_Futurism.jpg/330px-Manifesto_of_Futurism.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Manifesto_of_Futurism.jpg/440px-Manifesto_of_Futurism.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Manifesto_of_Futurism.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Filippo_Tommaso_Marinetti" title="Filippo Tommaso Marinetti">Filippo Tommaso Marinetti</a>, Italian modernist author of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Futurist_Manifesto" title="Futurist Manifesto">Futurist Manifesto</a> (1909) and later the co-author of the <a href="/wiki/Fascist_Manifesto" title="Fascist Manifesto">Fascist Manifesto</a> (1919)</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Futurism" title="Futurism">Futurism</a> was both an artistic-cultural movement and initially a political movement in Italy led by <a href="/wiki/Filippo_Tommaso_Marinetti" title="Filippo Tommaso Marinetti">Filippo Tommaso Marinetti</a> who founded the <a href="/wiki/Manifesto_of_Futurism" title="Manifesto of Futurism">Manifesto of Futurism</a> (1908), that championed the causes of modernism, action, and political violence as necessary elements of politics while denouncing liberalism and parliamentary politics. Marinetti rejected conventional democracy based on majority rule and egalitarianism, for a new form of democracy, promoting what he described in his work "The Futurist Conception of Democracy" as the following: "We are therefore able to give the directions to create and to dismantle to numbers, to quantity, to the mass, for with us number, quantity and mass will never be—as they are in Germany and Russia—the number, quantity and mass of mediocre men, incapable and indecisive."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHewitt1993153_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHewitt1993153-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>Futurism influenced fascism in its emphasis on recognizing the virile nature of violent action and war as being necessities of modern civilization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGori200414_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGori200414-113">[113]</a></sup> Marinetti promoted the need of physical training of young men saying that, in male education, gymnastics should take precedence over books. He advocated segregation of the genders because womanly sensibility must not enter men's education, which he claimed must be "lively, bellicose, muscular and violently dynamic."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGori200420–21_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGori200420–21-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Benito_Mussolini_1917.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="488" data-file-width="320" decoding="async" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Benito_Mussolini_1917.jpg/170px-Benito_Mussolini_1917.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Benito_Mussolini_1917.jpg/255px-Benito_Mussolini_1917.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ee/Benito_Mussolini_1917.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Benito_Mussolini_1917.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a> (here in 1917 as a soldier in <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>), who in 1914 founded and led the <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fasci_d%27Azione_Rivoluzionaria" title="Fasci d'Azione Rivoluzionaria">Fasci d'Azione Rivoluzionaria</a></i></span> to promote the Italian intervention in the war as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Revolutionary_nationalist" title="Revolutionary nationalist">revolutionary nationalist</a> action to liberate Italian-claimed lands from Austria-Hungary</div></div></div>
<h3><span id="World_War_I_and_its_aftermath_.281914.E2.80.931929.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_I_and_its_aftermath_(1914–1929)">World War I and its aftermath (1914–1929)</span></h3>
<p>At the outbreak of World War I in August 1914, the Italian political left became severely split over its position on the war. The <a href="/wiki/Italian_Socialist_Party" title="Italian Socialist Party">Italian Socialist Party</a> (PSI) opposed the war but a number of Italian revolutionary syndicalists supported war against Germany and Austria-Hungary on the grounds that their reactionary regimes had to be defeated to ensure the success of socialism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri1994175_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri1994175-115">[115]</a></sup> Angelo Oliviero Olivetti formed a pro-interventionist <a href="/wiki/Fascio" title="Fascio">fascio</a> called the <a href="/wiki/Fascio_Rivoluzionario_d%27Azione_Internazionalista" title="Fascio Rivoluzionario d'Azione Internazionalista">Revolutionary Fasces of International Action</a> in October 1914.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri1994175_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri1994175-115">[115]</a></sup> Benito Mussolini upon being expelled from his position as chief editor of the PSI's newspaper <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avanti!_(Italian_newspaper)" title="Avanti! (Italian newspaper)">Avanti!</a></i></span> for his anti-German stance, joined the interventionist cause in a separate fascio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri1994214_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri1994214-116">[116]</a></sup> The term "fascism" was first used in 1915 by members of Mussolini's movement, the Fasces of Revolutionary Action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO'Brien201452_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO'Brien201452-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first meeting of the Fasces of Revolutionary Action was held on 24 January 1915<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO'Brien201441_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO'Brien201441-118">[118]</a></sup> when Mussolini declared that it was necessary for Europe to resolve its national problems—including national borders—of Italy and elsewhere "for the ideals of justice and liberty for which oppressed peoples must acquire the right to belong to those national communities from which they descended."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO'Brien201441_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO'Brien201441-118">[118]</a></sup> Attempts to hold mass meetings were ineffective and the organization was regularly harassed by government authorities and socialists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregor1979195–196_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregor1979195–196-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IR_L%C3%BCbeck_033_-_EB.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1226" data-file-width="2498" decoding="async" height="108" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/IR_L%C3%BCbeck_033_-_EB.jpg/220px-IR_L%C3%BCbeck_033_-_EB.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/IR_L%C3%BCbeck_033_-_EB.jpg/330px-IR_L%C3%BCbeck_033_-_EB.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/IR_L%C3%BCbeck_033_-_EB.jpg/440px-IR_L%C3%BCbeck_033_-_EB.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:IR_L%C3%BCbeck_033_-_EB.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>German soldiers parading through Lübeck in the days leading up to World War I. <a href="/wiki/Johann_Plenge" title="Johann Plenge">Johann Plenge</a>'s concept of the "<a href="/wiki/Spirit_of_1914" title="Spirit of 1914">Spirit of 1914</a>" identified the outbreak of war as a moment that forged nationalistic German solidarity.</div></div></div>
<p>Similar political ideas arose in Germany after the outbreak of the war. German sociologist <a href="/wiki/Johann_Plenge" title="Johann Plenge">Johann Plenge</a> spoke of the rise of a "National Socialism" in Germany within what he termed the "ideas of 1914" that were a declaration of war against the "ideas of 1789" (the French Revolution).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitchen2006205_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitchen2006205-120">[120]</a></sup> According to Plenge, the "ideas of 1789"—such as the rights of man, democracy, individualism and liberalism—were being rejected in favor of "the ideas of 1914" that included "German values" of duty, discipline, law and order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitchen2006205_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitchen2006205-120">[120]</a></sup> Plenge believed that racial solidarity (<span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Volksgemeinschaft</i></span>) would replace class division and that "racial comrades" would unite to create a socialist society in the struggle of "proletarian" Germany against "capitalist" Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitchen2006205_120-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitchen2006205-120">[120]</a></sup> He believed that the <a href="/wiki/Spirit_of_1914" title="Spirit of 1914">Spirit of 1914</a> manifested itself in the concept of the People's League of National Socialism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHüppauf199792_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHüppauf199792-121">[121]</a></sup> This National Socialism was a form of state socialism that rejected the "idea of boundless freedom" and promoted an economy that would serve the whole of Germany under the leadership of the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHüppauf199792_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHüppauf199792-121">[121]</a></sup> This National Socialism was opposed to capitalism because of the components that were against "the national interest" of Germany but insisted that National Socialism would strive for greater efficiency in the economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHüppauf199792_121-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHüppauf199792-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeld1980_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeld1980-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (October 2021)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Plenge advocated an authoritarian rational ruling elite to develop National Socialism through a hierarchical <a href="/wiki/Technocracy" title="Technocracy">technocratic</a> state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERohkrämer2007130_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERohkrämer2007130-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_of_World_War_I">Impact of World War I</span></h4>
