<p>Fascists viewed World War I as bringing revolutionary changes in the nature of war, society, the state and technology, as the advent of <a href="/wiki/Total_war" title="Total war">total war</a> and mass mobilization had broken down the distinction between civilian and combatant, as civilians had become a critical part in economic production for the war effort and thus arose a "military citizenship" in which all citizens were involved to the military in some manner during the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlamires2006140–141,_670Mann200465_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlamires2006140–141,_670Mann200465-10">[10]</a></sup> World War I had resulted in the rise of a powerful state capable of mobilizing millions of people to serve on the front lines or provide economic production and logistics to support those on the front lines, as well as having unprecedented authority to intervene in the lives of citizens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlamires2006140–141,_670Mann200465_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlamires2006140–141,_670Mann200465-10">[10]</a></sup> Fascists viewed technological developments of weaponry and the state's total mobilization of its population in the war as symbolizing the beginning of a new era fusing state power with <a href="/wiki/Mass_politics" title="Mass politics">mass politics</a>, technology and particularly the mobilizing myth that they contended had triumphed over the myth of progress and the era of liberalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlamires2006140–141,_670_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlamires2006140–141,_670-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Italian_Arditi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="471" data-file-width="696" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Italian_Arditi.jpg/220px-Italian_Arditi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Italian_Arditi.jpg/330px-Italian_Arditi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Italian_Arditi.jpg/440px-Italian_Arditi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Italian_Arditi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Members of Italy's <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it"><a href="/wiki/Arditi" title="Arditi">Arditi</a></i></span> corps (here in 1918 holding daggers, a symbol of their group), which was formed in 1917 as groups of soldiers trained for dangerous missions, characterized by refusal to surrender and willingness to fight to the death. Their black uniforms inspired those of the Italian Fascist movement.</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_of_the_Bolshevik_Revolution">Impact of the Bolshevik Revolution</span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/October_Revolution" title="October Revolution">October Revolution</a> of 1917, in which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bolshevik" title="Bolshevik">Bolshevik</a> communists led by <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Vladimir Lenin</a> seized power in Russia, greatly influenced the development of fascism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlamires200695–96_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlamires200695–96-125">[125]</a></sup> In 1917, Mussolini, as leader of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fasces_of_Revolutionary_Action" title="Fasces of Revolutionary Action">Fasces of Revolutionary Action</a>, praised the October Revolution, but later he became unimpressed with Lenin, regarding him as merely a new version of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tsar_Nicholas_II" title="Tsar Nicholas II">Tsar Nicholas II</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeville200436_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeville200436-126">[126]</a></sup> After World War I, fascists commonly campaigned on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-Marxist" title="Anti-Marxist">anti-Marxist</a> agendas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlamires200695–96_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlamires200695–96-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>Liberal opponents of both fascism and the Bolsheviks argue that there are various similarities between the two, including that they believed in the necessity of a vanguard leadership, had disdain for bourgeois values, and it is argued had totalitarian ambitions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlamires200695–96_125-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlamires200695–96-125">[125]</a></sup> In practice, both have commonly emphasized revolutionary action, proletarian nation theories, one-party states, and party-armies;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlamires200695–96_125-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlamires200695–96-125">[125]</a></sup> however, both draw clear distinctions from each other both in aims and tactics, with the Bolsheviks emphasizing the need for an organized <a href="/wiki/Participatory_democracy" title="Participatory democracy">participatory democracy</a> (<a href="/wiki/Soviet_democracy" title="Soviet democracy">Soviet democracy</a>) and an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egalitarian" title="Egalitarian">egalitarian</a>, internationalist vision for society based on <a href="/wiki/Proletarian_internationalism" title="Proletarian internationalism">proletarian internationalism</a>, while fascists emphasized <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyper-nationalism" title="Hyper-nationalism">hyper-nationalism</a> and open hostility towards democracy, envisioning a hierarchical <a href="/wiki/Social_structure" title="Social structure">social structure</a> as essential to their aims. With the antagonism between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-interventionist" title="Anti-interventionist">anti-interventionist</a> Marxists and pro-<a href="/wiki/Interventionism_(politics)" title="Interventionism (politics)">interventionist</a> fascists complete by the end of the war, the two sides became irreconcilable. The fascists presented themselves as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-communists" title="Anti-communists">anti-communists</a> and as especially opposed to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marxists" title="Marxists">Marxists</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri1994178_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri1994178-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1919, Mussolini consolidated control over the fascist movement, known as <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it"><a href="/wiki/Sansepolcrismo" title="Sansepolcrismo">Sansepolcrismo</a></i></span>, with the founding of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_Fasces_of_Combat" title="Italian Fasces of Combat">Italian Fasces of Combat</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEncyclopedia_Britannica_''The_fascist_era''_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEncyclopedia_Britannica_''The_fascist_era''-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fascist_Manifesto_and_Charter_of_Carnaro">Fascist Manifesto and Charter of Carnaro</span></h4>
<p>In 1919, <a href="/wiki/Alceste_De_Ambris" title="Alceste De Ambris">Alceste De Ambris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Futurist" title="Futurist">futurist</a> movement leader <a href="/wiki/Filippo_Tommaso_Marinetti" title="Filippo Tommaso Marinetti">Filippo Tommaso Marinetti</a> created "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Manifesto_of_the_Italian_Fasces_of_Combat" title="The Manifesto of the Italian Fasces of Combat">The Manifesto of the Italian Fasces of Combat</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElazar200173_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElazar200173-128">[128]</a></sup> The Fascist Manifesto was presented on 6 June 1919 in the fascist newspaper <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it"><a href="/wiki/Il_Popolo_d%27Italia" title="Il Popolo d'Italia">Il Popolo d'Italia</a></i></span> and supported the creation of <a href="/wiki/Universal_suffrage" title="Universal suffrage">universal suffrage</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage" title="Women's suffrage">women's suffrage</a> (the latter being realized only partly in late 1925, with all opposition parties banned or disbanded);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPassmore2003116_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPassmore2003116-129">[129]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Proportional_representation" title="Proportional representation">proportional representation</a> on a regional basis; government representation through a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corporatist" title="Corporatist">corporatist</a> system of "National Councils" of experts, selected from professionals and tradespeople, elected to represent and hold legislative power over their respective areas, including labour, industry, transportation, public health, and communications, among others; and abolition of the <a href="/wiki/Senate_of_the_Kingdom_of_Italy" title="Senate of the Kingdom of Italy">Senate of the Kingdom of Italy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBorsellaCaso200769_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBorsellaCaso200769-130">[130]</a></sup> The Fascist Manifesto supported the creation of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eight-hour_work_day" title="Eight-hour work day">eight-hour work day</a> for all workers, a <a href="/wiki/Minimum_wage" title="Minimum wage">minimum wage</a>, worker representation in industrial management, equal confidence in labour unions as in industrial executives and public servants, reorganization of the transportation sector, revision of the draft law on invalidity insurance, reduction of the retirement age from 65 to 55, a strong <a href="/wiki/Progressive_tax" title="Progressive tax">progressive tax</a> on capital, confiscation of the property of religious institutions and abolishment of bishoprics, and revision of military contracts to allow the government to seize 85% of profits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBorsellaCaso200769–70_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBorsellaCaso200769–70-131">[131]</a></sup> It also called for the fulfillment of expansionist aims in the Balkans and other parts of the Mediterranean,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaxton2005_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaxton2005-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (October 2021)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> the creation of a short-service national militia to serve defensive duties, <a href="/wiki/Nationalization" title="Nationalization">nationalization</a> of the armaments industry, and a foreign policy designed to be peaceful but also competitive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBorsellaCaso200770_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBorsellaCaso200770-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fiume_cheering_D%27Annunzio.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="361" data-file-width="582" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Fiume_cheering_D%27Annunzio.jpg/220px-Fiume_cheering_D%27Annunzio.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Fiume_cheering_D%27Annunzio.jpg/330px-Fiume_cheering_D%27Annunzio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Fiume_cheering_D%27Annunzio.jpg/440px-Fiume_cheering_D%27Annunzio.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fiume_cheering_D%27Annunzio.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Residents of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fiume" title="Fiume">Fiume</a> cheer the arrival of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gabriele_d%27Annunzio" title="Gabriele d'Annunzio">Gabriele d'Annunzio</a> and his blackshirt-wearing nationalist raiders, as D'Annunzio and fascist <a href="/wiki/Alceste_De_Ambris" title="Alceste De Ambris">Alceste De Ambris</a> developed the quasi-fascist <a href="/wiki/Italian_Regency_of_Carnaro" title="Italian Regency of Carnaro">Italian Regency of Carnaro</a> (a city-state in Fiume) from 1919 to 1920 and whose actions by D'Annunzio in Fiume inspired the Italian fascist movement.</div></div></div>
<p>The next events that influenced the fascists in Italy was the raid of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fiume_(city)" title="Fiume (city)">Fiume</a> by Italian nationalist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gabriele_d%27Annunzio" title="Gabriele d'Annunzio">Gabriele d'Annunzio</a> and the founding of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charter_of_Carnaro" title="Charter of Carnaro">Charter of Carnaro</a> in 1920.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri1994186_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri1994186-134">[134]</a></sup> D'Annunzio and De Ambris designed the Charter, which advocated national-syndicalist corporatist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Productionism" title="Productionism">productionism</a> alongside D'Annunzio's political views.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri1994187_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri1994187-135">[135]</a></sup> Many fascists saw the Charter of Carnaro as an ideal constitution for a fascist Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri1994189_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri1994189-136">[136]</a></sup> This behaviour of aggression towards Yugoslavia and <a href="/wiki/South_Slavs" title="South Slavs">South Slavs</a> was pursued by Italian fascists with their persecution of South Slavs—especially Slovenes and Croats.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="From_populism_to_conservative_accommodations">From populism to conservative accommodations</span></h4>
<p>In 1920, militant strike activity by industrial workers reached its peak in Italy and 1919 and 1920 were known as the "Red Year" (<span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it"><a href="/wiki/Biennio_Rosso" title="Biennio Rosso">Biennio Rosso</a></i></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBorsellaCaso200773_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBorsellaCaso200773-137">[137]</a></sup> Mussolini and the fascists took advantage of the situation by allying with industrial businesses and attacking workers and peasants in the name of preserving order and internal peace in Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBorsellaCaso200775_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBorsellaCaso200775-138">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fascists identified their primary opponents as the majority of socialists on the left who had opposed intervention in World War I.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri1994189_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri1994189-136">[136]</a></sup> The fascists and the Italian political right held common ground: both held Marxism in contempt, discounted class consciousness and believed in the rule of elites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri1994193_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri1994193-139">[139]</a></sup> The fascists assisted the anti-socialist campaign by allying with the other parties and the conservative right in a mutual effort to destroy the Italian Socialist Party and labour organizations committed to class identity above national identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri1994193_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri1994193-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fascism sought to accommodate Italian conservatives by making major alterations to its political agenda—abandoning its previous populism, <a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">republicanism</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anticlericalism" title="Anticlericalism">anticlericalism</a>, adopting policies in support of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_enterprise" title="Free enterprise">free enterprise</a> and accepting the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> and the monarchy as institutions in Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_Grand2000145_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_Grand2000145-140">[140]</a></sup> To appeal to Italian conservatives, fascism adopted policies such as promoting family values, including policies designed to reduce the number of women in the workforce—limiting the woman's role to that of a mother. The fascists banned literature on birth control and increased penalties for abortion in 1926, declaring both crimes against the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlinkhorn200314_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlinkhorn200314-141">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although fascism adopted a number of anti-modern positions designed to appeal to people upset with the new trends in sexuality and women's rights—especially those with a <a href="/wiki/Reactionary" title="Reactionary">reactionary</a> point of view—the fascists sought to maintain fascism's revolutionary character, with Angelo Oliviero Olivetti saying: "Fascism would like to be conservative, but it will [be] by being revolutionary."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri1994190_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESternhellSznajderAshéri1994190-142">[142]</a></sup> The Fascists supported revolutionary action and committed to secure law and order to appeal to both conservatives and syndicalists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlinkhorn200322_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlinkhorn200322-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>Prior to fascism's accommodations to the political right, fascism was a small, urban, northern Italian movement that had about a thousand members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBorsellaCaso200772_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBorsellaCaso200772-144">[144]</a></sup> After Fascism's accommodation of the political right, the fascist movement's membership soared to approximately 250,000 by 1921.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBorsellaCaso200776_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBorsellaCaso200776-145">[145]</a></sup> A 2020 article by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daron_Acemo%C4%9Flu" title="Daron Acemoğlu">Daron Acemoğlu</a>, Giuseppe De Feo, Giacomo De Luca, and Gianluca Russo in the <a href="/wiki/Center_for_Economic_and_Policy_Research" title="Center for Economic and Policy Research">Center for Economic and Policy Research</a>, exploring the link between the threat of <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialism</a> and Mussolini's rise to power, found "a strong association between the Red Scare in Italy and the subsequent local support for the Fascist Party in the early 1920s." According to the authors, it was local elites and large landowners who played an important role in boosting Fascist Party activity and support, which did not come from socialists' core supporters but from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centre-right" title="Centre-right">centre-right</a> voters, as they viewed traditional centre-right parties as ineffective in stopping socialism and turned to the Fascists. In 2003, historian Adrian Lyttelton wrote: "The expansion of Fascism in the rural areas was stimulated and directed by the reaction of the farmers and landowners against the peasant leagues of both Socialists and Catholics."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAcemoğluDe_FeoDe_LucaRusso2020_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAcemoğluDe_FeoDe_LucaRusso2020-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fascist_violence">Fascist violence</span></h4>
<p>Beginning in 1922, fascist paramilitaries escalated their strategy from one of attacking socialist offices and the homes of socialist leadership figures, to one of violent occupation of cities. The fascists met little serious resistance from authorities and proceeded to take over several northern Italian cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaxton200587_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaxton200587-147">[147]</a></sup> The fascists attacked the headquarters of socialist and Catholic labour unions in Cremona and imposed forced Italianization upon the German-speaking population of Trent and Bolzano.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaxton200587_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaxton200587-147">[147]</a></sup> After seizing these cities, the fascists made plans to take <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaxton200587_147-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaxton200587-147">[147]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:March_on_Rome.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="931" data-file-width="1247" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/March_on_Rome.jpg/220px-March_on_Rome.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/March_on_Rome.jpg/330px-March_on_Rome.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/March_on_Rome.jpg/440px-March_on_Rome.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:March_on_Rome.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a> with three of the four <a href="/wiki/Quadrumvirs" title="Quadrumvirs">quadrumvirs</a> during the <a href="/wiki/March_on_Rome" title="March on Rome">March on Rome</a> (from left to right: unknown, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emilio_de_Bono" title="Emilio de Bono">de Bono</a>, Mussolini, <a href="/wiki/Italo_Balbo" title="Italo Balbo">Balbo</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cesare_Maria_de_Vecchi" title="Cesare Maria de Vecchi">de Vecchi</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>On 24 October 1922, the Fascist Party held its annual congress in <a href="/wiki/Naples" title="Naples">Naples</a>, where Mussolini ordered Blackshirts to take control of public buildings and trains and to converge on three points around Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaxton200587_147-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaxton200587-147">[147]</a></sup> The Fascists managed to seize control of several post offices and trains in northern Italy while the Italian government, led by a left-wing coalition, was internally divided and unable to respond to the Fascist advances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaxton200588_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaxton200588-148">[148]</a></sup> King Victor Emmanuel III of Italy perceived the risk of bloodshed in Rome in response to attempting to disperse the Fascists to be too high.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaxton200590_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaxton200590-149">[149]</a></sup> Victor Emmanuel III decided to appoint Mussolini as <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Italy" title="Prime Minister of Italy">Prime Minister of Italy</a> and Mussolini arrived in Rome on 30 October to accept the appointment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaxton200590_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaxton200590-149">[149]</a></sup> Fascist propaganda aggrandized this event, known as "<a href="/wiki/March_on_Rome" title="March on Rome">March on Rome</a>", as a "seizure" of power because of Fascists' heroic exploits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaxton200587_147-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaxton200587-147">[147]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fascist_Italy">Fascist Italy</span></h3>
<p>Historian Stanley G. Payne says: "[Fascism in Italy was a] primarily political dictatorship. ... The Fascist Party itself had become almost completely bureaucratized and subservient to, not dominant over, the state itself. Big business, industry, and finance retained extensive autonomy, particularly in the early years. The armed forces also enjoyed considerable autonomy. ... The Fascist militia was placed under military control. ... The judicial system was left largely intact and relatively autonomous as well. The police continued to be directed by state officials and were not taken over by party leaders ... nor was a major new police elite created. ... There was never any question of bringing the Church under overall subservience. ... Sizable sectors of Italian cultural life retained extensive autonomy, and no major state propaganda-and-culture ministry existed. ... The Mussolini regime was neither especially sanguinary nor particularly repressive."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayne1995122_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayne1995122-150">[150]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mussolini_in_power">Mussolini in power</span></h4>
<p>Upon being appointed Prime Minister of Italy, Mussolini had to form a coalition government because the Fascists did not have control over the Italian parliament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayne1995110_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayne1995110-151">[151]</a></sup> Mussolini's coalition government initially pursued <a href="/wiki/Economic_liberalism" title="Economic liberalism">economically liberal</a> policies under the direction of liberal finance minister <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alberto_De_Stefani" title="Alberto De Stefani">Alberto De Stefani</a>, a member of the Center Party, including balancing the budget through deep cuts to the civil service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayne1995110_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayne1995110-151">[151]</a></sup> Initially, little drastic change in government policy had occurred and repressive police actions were limited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayne1995110_151-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayne1995110-151">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Fascists began their attempt to entrench fascism in Italy with the <a href="/wiki/Acerbo_Law" title="Acerbo Law">Acerbo Law</a>, which guaranteed a plurality of the seats in parliament to any party or coalition list in an election that received 25% or more of the vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayne1995113_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayne1995113-152">[152]</a></sup> Through considerable Fascist violence and intimidation, the list won a majority of the vote, allowing many seats to go to the Fascists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayne1995113_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayne1995113-152">[152]</a></sup> In the aftermath of the election, a crisis and political scandal erupted after Socialist Party deputy <a href="/wiki/Giacomo_Matteotti" title="Giacomo Matteotti">Giacomo Matteotti</a> was kidnapped and murdered by a Fascist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayne1995113_152-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayne1995113-152">[152]</a></sup> The liberals and the leftist minority in parliament walked out in protest in what became known as the <a href="/wiki/Aventine_Secession_(20th_century)" title="Aventine Secession (20th century)">Aventine Secession</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayne1995114_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayne1995114-153">[153]</a></sup> On 3 January 1925, Mussolini addressed the Fascist-dominated Italian parliament and declared that he was personally responsible for what happened, but insisted that he had done nothing wrong. Mussolini proclaimed himself dictator of Italy, assuming full responsibility over the government and announcing the dismissal of parliament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayne1995114_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayne1995114-153">[153]</a></sup> From 1925 to 1929, fascism steadily became entrenched in power: opposition deputies were denied access to parliament, censorship was introduced and a December 1925 decree made Mussolini solely responsible to the King.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayne1995115_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayne1995115-154">[154]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Catholic_Church">Catholic Church</span></h4>
<p>In 1929, the Fascist regime briefly gained what was in effect a blessing of the Catholic Church after the regime signed a concordat with the Church, known as the <a href="/wiki/Lateran_Treaty" title="Lateran Treaty">Lateran Treaty</a>, which gave the papacy state sovereignty and financial compensation for the seizure of Church lands by the liberal state in the 19th century, but within two years the Church had renounced fascism in the Encyclical <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non_Abbiamo_Bisogno" title="Non Abbiamo Bisogno">Non Abbiamo Bisogno</a></i> as a "pagan idolatry of the state" which teaches "hatred, violence and irreverence".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayne1995119–120_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayne1995119–120-155">[155]</a></sup> Not long after signing the agreement, by Mussolini's own confession, the Church had threatened to have him "excommunicated", in part because of his intractable nature, but also because he had "confiscated more issues of Catholic newspapers in the next three months than in the previous seven years."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMack_Smith1983162_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMack_Smith1983162-156">[156]</a></sup> By the late 1930s, Mussolini became more vocal in his anti-clerical rhetoric, repeatedly denouncing the Catholic Church and discussing ways to depose the pope. He took the position that the "papacy was a malignant tumor in the body of Italy and must 'be rooted out once and for all,' because there was no room in Rome for both the Pope and himself."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMack_Smith1983222–223_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMack_Smith1983222–223-157">[157]</a></sup> In her 1974 book, Mussolini's widow Rachele stated that her husband had always been an atheist until near the end of his life, writing that her husband was "basically irreligious until the later years of his life."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMussolini1977131_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMussolini1977131-158">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Nazis in Germany employed similar anti-clerical policies. The Gestapo confiscated hundreds of monasteries in Austria and Germany, evicted clergymen and laymen alike and often replaced crosses with swastikas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Lang1979221_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Lang1979221-159">[159]</a></sup> Referring to the swastika as "the Devil's Cross", church leaders found their youth organizations banned, their meetings limited and various Catholic periodicals censored or banned. Government officials eventually found it necessary to place "Nazis into editorial positions in the Catholic press."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2005239_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2005239-160">[160]</a></sup> Up to 2,720 clerics, mostly Catholics, were arrested by the Gestapo and imprisoned inside of Germany's Dachau concentration camp, resulting in over 1,000 deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBerben1975276–277_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBerben1975276–277-161">[161]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Corporatist_economic_system">Corporatist economic system</span></h4>
<p>The Fascist regime created a corporatist economic system in 1925 with creation of <a href="/wiki/Pact_of_the_Vidoni_Palace" title="Pact of the Vidoni Palace">the Palazzo Vidoni Pact</a>, in which the Italian employers' association <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confindustria" title="Confindustria">Confindustria</a> and fascist trade unions agreed to recognize each other as the sole representatives of Italy's employers and employees, excluding non-fascist trade unions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlamires2006150_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlamires2006150-162">[162]</a></sup> The Fascist regime first created a Ministry of Corporations that organized the Italian economy into 22 sectoral corporations, banned workers' strikes and lock-outs and in 1927 created the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Charter_of_1927" title="Labour Charter of 1927">Charter of Labour</a>, which established workers' rights and duties and created labour tribunals to arbitrate employer-employee disputes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlamires2006150_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlamires2006150-162">[162]</a></sup> In practice, the sectoral corporations exercised little independence and were largely controlled by the regime, and the employee organizations were rarely led by employees themselves, but instead by appointed Fascist party members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlamires2006150_162-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlamires2006150-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Aggressive_foreign_policy">Aggressive foreign policy</span></h4>
<p>In the 1920s, Fascist Italy pursued an aggressive foreign policy that included an attack on the Greek island of <a href="/wiki/Corfu" title="Corfu">Corfu</a>, ambitions to expand Italian territory in the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>, plans to wage war against <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Yugoslavia" title="Kingdom of Yugoslavia">Yugoslavia</a>, attempts to bring Yugoslavia into civil war by supporting Croat and Macedonian separatists to legitimize Italian intervention and making <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a> a <i>de facto</i> <a href="/wiki/Protectorate" title="Protectorate">protectorate</a> of Italy, which was achieved through diplomatic means by 1927.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKallis2000132_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKallis2000132-163">[163]</a></sup> In response to revolt in the Italian colony of <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>, Fascist Italy abandoned previous liberal-era colonial policy of cooperation with local leaders. Instead, claiming that Italians were a superior race to African races and thereby had the right to colonize the "inferior" Africans, it sought to settle 10 to 15 million Italians in Libya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAhmida1994134–135_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAhmida1994134–135-164">[164]</a></sup> This resulted in an aggressive military campaign known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacification_of_Libya" title="Pacification of Libya">Pacification of Libya</a> against natives in Libya, including mass killings, the use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concentration_camp" title="Concentration camp">concentration camps</a> and the forced starvation of thousands of people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAhmida1994134–135_164-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAhmida1994134–135-164">[164]</a></sup> Italian authorities committed <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_cleansing" title="Ethnic cleansing">ethnic cleansing</a> by forcibly expelling 100,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bedouins" title="Bedouins">Bedouin</a> Cyrenaicans, half the population of Cyrenaica in Libya, from their settlements that was slated to be given to Italian settlers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECardoza2006109BloxhamMoses2010358_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECardoza2006109BloxhamMoses2010358-165">[165]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hitler_adopts_Italian_model">Hitler adopts Italian model</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_119-1486,_Hitler-Putsch,_M%C3%BCnchen,_Marienplatz.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="467" data-file-width="790" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Bundesarchiv_Bild_119-1486%2C_Hitler-Putsch%2C_M%C3%BCnchen%2C_Marienplatz.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_119-1486%2C_Hitler-Putsch%2C_M%C3%BCnchen%2C_Marienplatz.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Bundesarchiv_Bild_119-1486%2C_Hitler-Putsch%2C_M%C3%BCnchen%2C_Marienplatz.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_119-1486%2C_Hitler-Putsch%2C_M%C3%BCnchen%2C_Marienplatz.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Bundesarchiv_Bild_119-1486%2C_Hitler-Putsch%2C_M%C3%BCnchen%2C_Marienplatz.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_119-1486%2C_Hitler-Putsch%2C_M%C3%BCnchen%2C_Marienplatz.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_119-1486,_Hitler-Putsch,_M%C3%BCnchen,_Marienplatz.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nazis in Munich during the <a href="/wiki/Beer_Hall_Putsch" title="Beer Hall Putsch">Beer Hall Putsch</a></div></div></div>
<p>The March on Rome brought fascism international attention. One early admirer of the Italian Fascists was <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a>, who less than a month after the March had begun to model himself and the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Party" title="Nazi Party">Nazi Party</a> upon Mussolini and the Fascists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw2000182_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw2000182-166">[166]</a></sup> The Nazis, led by Hitler and the German war hero <a href="/wiki/Erich_Ludendorff" title="Erich Ludendorff">Erich Ludendorff</a>, attempted a "March on Berlin" modeled upon the March on Rome, which resulted in the failed <a href="/wiki/Beer_Hall_Putsch" title="Beer Hall Putsch">Beer Hall Putsch</a> in <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a> in November 1923.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJablonsky198920–26,_30_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJablonsky198920–26,_30-167">[167]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_impact_of_the_Great_Depression_and_buildup_to_World_War_II">International impact of the Great Depression and buildup to World War II</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hitlermusso2_edit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1100" data-file-width="697" decoding="async" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b1/Hitlermusso2_edit.jpg/170px-Hitlermusso2_edit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b1/Hitlermusso2_edit.jpg/255px-Hitlermusso2_edit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b1/Hitlermusso2_edit.jpg/340px-Hitlermusso2_edit.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hitlermusso2_edit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a> (left) and <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> (right)</div></div></div>
<p>The conditions of economic hardship caused by the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> brought about an international surge of social unrest. According to historian Philip Morgan, "the onset of the Great Depression ... was the greatest stimulus yet to the diffusion and expansion of fascism outside Italy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan2003_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan2003-168">[168]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (October 2021)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Fascist propaganda blamed the problems of the long depression of the 1930s on minorities and <a href="/wiki/Scapegoat" title="Scapegoat">scapegoats</a>: "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-semitism" title="Anti-semitism">Judeo</a>-<a href="/wiki/Freemasonry" title="Freemasonry">Masonic</a>-<a href="/wiki/Bolshevism" title="Bolshevism">bolshevik</a>" conspiracies, <a href="/wiki/Left-wing_politics#Nationalism,_anti-imperialism_and_anti-nationalism" title="Left-wing politics">left-wing internationalism</a> and the presence of immigrants.
</p><p>In Germany, it contributed to the rise of the Nazi Party, which resulted in the demise of the <a href="/wiki/Weimar_Republic" title="Weimar Republic">Weimar Republic</a> and the establishment of the fascist regime, <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>, under the leadership of Adolf Hitler. With the rise of Hitler and the Nazis to power in 1933, <a href="/wiki/Liberal_democracy" title="Liberal democracy">liberal democracy</a> was dissolved in Germany and the Nazis mobilized the country for war, with expansionist territorial aims against several countries. In the 1930s, the Nazis implemented racial laws that deliberately discriminated against, <a href="/wiki/Disfranchisement" title="Disfranchisement">disenfranchised</a> and persecuted Jews and other racial and minority groups.
</p><p>Fascist movements grew in strength elsewhere in Europe. Hungarian fascist <a href="/wiki/Gyula_G%C3%B6mb%C3%B6s" title="Gyula Gömbös">Gyula Gömbös</a> rose to power as Prime Minister of <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a> in 1932 and attempted to entrench his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Party_of_Hungarian_Life" title="Party of Hungarian Life">Party of National Unity</a> throughout the country. He created an eight-hour work day and a forty-eight-hour work week in industry; sought to entrench a corporatist economy; and pursued irredentist claims on Hungary's neighbors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayne1995270_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayne1995270-169">[169]</a></sup> The fascist <a href="/wiki/Iron_Guard" title="Iron Guard">Iron Guard</a> movement in <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a> soared in political support after 1933, gaining representation in the Romanian government, and an Iron Guard member assassinated Romanian prime minister <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ion_Duca" title="Ion Duca">Ion Duca</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayne1995282–288_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayne1995282–288-170">[170]</a></sup> The Iron Guard was the only fascist movement outside Germany and Italy to <a href="/wiki/National_Legionary_State" title="National Legionary State">come to power</a> without foreign assistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/6_February_1934_crisis" title="6 February 1934 crisis">6 February 1934 crisis</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> faced the greatest domestic political turmoil since the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dreyfus_Affair" title="Dreyfus Affair">Dreyfus Affair</a> when the fascist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Francist_Movement" title="Francist Movement">Francist Movement</a> and multiple far-right movements rioted <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/En_masse" title="En masse">en masse</a></i> in Paris against the French government resulting in major political violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoolf1983311_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoolf1983311-173">[173]</a></sup> A variety of <b>para-fascist</b> governments that borrowed elements from fascism were formed during the Great Depression, including those of <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> and Yugoslavia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayne1995145_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayne1995145-174">[174]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SaudacaoIntegralista1935.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="469" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/SaudacaoIntegralista1935.jpg/170px-SaudacaoIntegralista1935.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/SaudacaoIntegralista1935.jpg/255px-SaudacaoIntegralista1935.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/SaudacaoIntegralista1935.jpg/340px-SaudacaoIntegralista1935.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SaudacaoIntegralista1935.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Integralism" title="Brazilian Integralism">Integralists</a> marching in Brazil</div></div></div>
<p>In the Americas, the <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Integralism" title="Brazilian Integralism">Brazilian Integralists</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Pl%C3%ADnio_Salgado" title="Plínio Salgado">Plínio Salgado</a> claimed as many as 200,000 members, although following coup attempts it faced a crackdown from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Estado_Novo_(Brazil)" title="Estado Novo (Brazil)">Estado Novo</a> of <a href="/wiki/Get%C3%BAlio_Vargas" title="Getúlio Vargas">Getúlio Vargas</a> in 1937.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriffin1991150–152_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriffin1991150–152-175">[175]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>, the fascist <a href="/wiki/Revolutionary_Union_(Peru)" title="Revolutionary Union (Peru)">Revolutionary Union</a> was a fascist political party which was in power 1931 to 1933. In the 1930s, the <a href="/wiki/National_Socialist_Movement_of_Chile" title="National Socialist Movement of Chile">National Socialist Movement of Chile</a> gained seats in <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>'s parliament and attempted a coup d'état that resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Seguro_Obrero_massacre" title="Seguro Obrero massacre">Seguro Obrero massacre</a> of 1938.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayne1995341–342_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayne1995341–342-176">[176]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the Great Depression, Mussolini promoted active state intervention in the economy. He denounced the contemporary "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supercapitalism_(concept_in_Italian_Fascism)" title="Supercapitalism (concept in Italian Fascism)">supercapitalism</a>" that he claimed began in 1914 as a failure because of its alleged <a href="/wiki/Decadence" title="Decadence">decadence</a>, its support for unlimited <a href="/wiki/Consumerism" title="Consumerism">consumerism</a>, and its intention to create the "standardization of humankind."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBerghaus2000136–137_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBerghaus2000136–137-177">[177]</a></sup> Fascist Italy created the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Institute_for_Industrial_Reconstruction" title="Institute for Industrial Reconstruction">Institute for Industrial Reconstruction</a> (IRI), a giant state-owned firm and holding company that provided state funding to failing private enterprises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlamires2006189_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlamires2006189-178">[178]</a></sup> The IRI was made a permanent institution in Fascist Italy in 1937, pursued fascist policies to create national <a href="/wiki/Autarky" title="Autarky">autarky</a> and had the power to take over private firms to maximize war production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlamires2006189_178-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlamires2006189-178">[178]</a></sup> While Hitler's regime only nationalized 500 companies in key industries by the early 1940s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery199416_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery199416-179">[179]</a></sup> Mussolini declared in 1934 that "[t]hree-fourths of Italian economy, industrial and agricultural, is in the hands of the state."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> Due to the worldwide depression, Mussolini's government was able to take over most of Italy's largest failing banks, who held controlling interest in many Italian businesses. The Institute for Industrial Reconstruction, a state-operated holding company in charge of bankrupt banks and companies, reported in early 1934 that they held assets of "48.5 percent of the share capital of Italy", which later included the capital of the banks themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEToniolo201359_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEToniolo201359-181">[181]</a></sup> Political historian Martin Blinkhorn estimated Italy's scope of state intervention and ownership "greatly surpassed that in Nazi Germany, giving Italy a public sector second only to that of Stalin's Russia."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlinkhorn200646_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlinkhorn200646-182">[182]</a></sup> In the late 1930s, Italy enacted manufacturing cartels, tariff barriers, currency restrictions and massive regulation of the economy to attempt to balance payments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlamires200672_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlamires200672-183">[183]</a></sup> Italy's policy of autarky failed to achieve effective economic autonomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlamires200672_183-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlamires200672-183">[183]</a></sup> Nazi Germany similarly pursued an economic agenda with the aims of autarky and rearmament and imposed <a href="/wiki/Protectionism" title="Protectionism">protectionist</a> policies, including forcing the German steel industry to use lower-quality German iron ore rather than superior-quality imported iron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlamires2006190_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlamires2006190-184">[184]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="World_War_II_.281939.E2.80.931945.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II_(1939–1945)">World War II (1939–1945)</span></h3>
<p>In Fascist Italy and Nazi Germany, both Mussolini and Hitler pursued territorial expansionist and <a href="/wiki/Interventionism_(politics)" title="Interventionism (politics)">interventionist foreign policy</a> agendas from the 1930s through the 1940s culminating in World War II. Mussolini called for <a href="/wiki/Irredentism" title="Irredentism">irredentist</a> Italian claims to be reclaimed, establishing Italian domination of the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a> and securing Italian access to the Atlantic Ocean and the creation of Italian <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it"><a href="/wiki/Spazio_vitale" title="Spazio vitale">spazio vitale</a></i></span> ("vital space") in the Mediterranean and <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKallis200051_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKallis200051-185">[185]</a></sup> Hitler called for irredentist German claims to be reclaimed along with the creation of German <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Lebensraum" title="Lebensraum">Lebensraum</a></i></span> ("living space") in Eastern Europe, including territories held by the Soviet Union, that would be colonized by Germans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKallis200053_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKallis200053-186">[186]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inmate_at_the_Rab_concentration_camp.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="424" data-file-width="644" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Inmate_at_the_Rab_concentration_camp.jpg/220px-Inmate_at_the_Rab_concentration_camp.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Inmate_at_the_Rab_concentration_camp.jpg/330px-Inmate_at_the_Rab_concentration_camp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Inmate_at_the_Rab_concentration_camp.jpg/440px-Inmate_at_the_Rab_concentration_camp.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Inmate_at_the_Rab_concentration_camp.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Emaciation" title="Emaciation">Emaciated</a> male inmate at the Italian <a href="/wiki/Rab_concentration_camp" title="Rab concentration camp">Rab concentration camp</a></div></div></div>
<p>From 1935 to 1939, Germany and Italy escalated their demands for territorial claims and greater influence in world affairs. Italy <a href="/wiki/Second_Italo-Ethiopian_War" title="Second Italo-Ethiopian War">invaded Ethiopia in 1935</a> resulting in its condemnation by the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> and its widespread diplomatic isolation. In 1936, Germany <a href="/wiki/Remilitarization_of_the_Rhineland" title="Remilitarization of the Rhineland">remilitarized the industrial Rhineland</a>, a region that had been ordered demilitarized by the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Versailles" title="Treaty of Versailles">Treaty of Versailles</a>. In 1938, Germany annexed <a href="/wiki/Federal_State_of_Austria" title="Federal State of Austria">Austria</a> and Italy assisted Germany in resolving the diplomatic crisis between Germany versus Britain and France over claims on <a href="/wiki/Czechoslovakia" title="Czechoslovakia">Czechoslovakia</a> by arranging the <a href="/wiki/Munich_Agreement" title="Munich Agreement">Munich Agreement</a> that gave Germany the <a href="/wiki/Sudetenland" title="Sudetenland">Sudetenland</a> and was perceived at the time to have averted a European war. These hopes faded when Czechoslovakia was dissolved by the proclamation of the German client state of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slovak_State" title="Slovak State">Slovakia</a>, followed by the next day of the occupation of the remaining <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Czech_Lands" title="Czech Lands">Czech Lands</a> and the proclamation of the German <a href="/wiki/Protectorate_of_Bohemia_and_Moravia" title="Protectorate of Bohemia and Moravia">Protectorate of Bohemia and Moravia</a>. At the same time from 1938 to 1939, Italy was demanding territorial and colonial concessions from France and Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERodogno200647_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERodogno200647-187">[187]</a></sup> In 1939, Germany prepared for war with Poland, but attempted to gain territorial concessions from Poland through diplomatic means.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson2004371–372_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson2004371–372-188">[188]</a></sup> The Polish government did not trust Hitler's promises and refused to accept Germany's demands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson2004371–372_188-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson2004371–372-188">[188]</a></sup>
</p><p>The invasion of Poland by Germany was deemed unacceptable by Britain, France and their allies, resulting in their mutual declaration of war against Germany that was deemed the aggressor in the war in Poland, resulting in the outbreak of World War II. In 1940, Mussolini led Italy into World War II on the side of the Axis. Mussolini was aware that Italy did not have the military capacity to carry out a long war with France or the United Kingdom and waited until France was on the verge of imminent collapse and surrender from the German invasion before declaring war on France and the United Kingdom on 10 June 1940 on the assumption that the war would be short-lived following France's collapse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKnox1999122–123_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKnox1999122–123-189">[189]</a></sup> Mussolini believed that following a brief entry of Italy into war with France, followed by the imminent French surrender, Italy could gain some territorial concessions from France and then concentrate its forces on a major offensive in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> where British and Commonwealth forces were outnumbered by Italian forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKnox1999122–127_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKnox1999122–127-190">[190]</a></sup> Plans by Germany to invade the United Kingdom in 1940 failed after Germany lost the aerial warfare campaign in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain" title="Battle of Britain">Battle of Britain</a>. In 1941, the Axis campaign spread to the Soviet Union after Hitler launched <a href="/wiki/Operation_Barbarossa" title="Operation Barbarossa">Operation Barbarossa</a>. Axis forces at the height of their power controlled almost all of continental Europe. The war became prolonged—contrary to Mussolini's plans—resulting in Italy losing battles on multiple fronts and requiring German assistance.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Buchenwald_Corpses_60623.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="944" data-file-width="1400" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Buchenwald_Corpses_60623.jpg/220px-Buchenwald_Corpses_60623.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Buchenwald_Corpses_60623.jpg/330px-Buchenwald_Corpses_60623.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Buchenwald_Corpses_60623.jpg/440px-Buchenwald_Corpses_60623.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Buchenwald_Corpses_60623.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Corpses of victims of the German <a href="/wiki/Buchenwald_concentration_camp" title="Buchenwald concentration camp">Buchenwald concentration camp</a></div></div></div>
<p>During World War II, the Axis Powers in Europe led by Nazi Germany participated in the extermination of millions of Poles, Jews, Gypsies and others in the genocide known as the Holocaust.
</p><p>After 1942, Axis forces began to falter. In 1943, after Italy faced multiple military failures, the complete reliance and subordination of Italy to Germany, the Allied invasion of Italy and the corresponding international humiliation, Mussolini <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/25_Luglio" title="25 Luglio">was removed as head of government and arrested</a> on the order of King Victor Emmanuel III, who proceeded to dismantle the Fascist state and declared Italy's switching of allegiance to the Allied side. Mussolini was rescued from arrest by German forces and led the German client state, the Italian Social Republic from 1943 to 1945. Nazi Germany faced multiple losses and steady Soviet and Western Allied offensives from 1943 to 1945.
</p><p>On 28 April 1945, Mussolini was captured and executed by Italian communist partisans. On 30 April 1945, Hitler committed suicide. Shortly afterwards, Germany surrendered and the Nazi regime was <a href="/wiki/Denazification" title="Denazification">systematically dismantled</a> by the occupying Allied powers. An International Military Tribunal was subsequently convened in <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg#Nazi_era" title="Nuremberg">Nuremberg</a>. Beginning in November 1945 and lasting through 1949, numerous Nazi political, military and economic leaders were <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_trials" title="Nuremberg trials">tried and convicted</a> of <a href="/wiki/War_crime" title="War crime">war crimes</a>, with many of the worst offenders being sentenced to death and executed.
</p>
<h3><span id="Post-World_War_II_.281945.E2.80.932008.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-World_War_II_(1945–2008)">Post-World War II (1945–2008)</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Neo-fascism" title="Neo-fascism">Neo-fascism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Juan_Peron_con_banda_de_presidente.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="387" data-file-width="340" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Juan_Peron_con_banda_de_presidente.jpg/170px-Juan_Peron_con_banda_de_presidente.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Juan_Peron_con_banda_de_presidente.jpg/255px-Juan_Peron_con_banda_de_presidente.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/98/Juan_Peron_con_banda_de_presidente.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Juan_Peron_con_banda_de_presidente.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Juan_Per%C3%B3n" title="Juan Perón">Juan Perón</a>, <a href="/wiki/President_of_Argentina" title="President of Argentina">President of Argentina</a> from 1946 to 1955 and 1973 to 1974, admired <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_Fascism" title="Italian Fascism">Italian Fascism</a> and modelled his economic policies on those pursued by Fascist Italy.</div></div></div>
<p>The victory of the Allies over the Axis powers in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> led to the collapse of many fascist regimes in Europe. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuremberg_Trials" title="Nuremberg Trials">Nuremberg Trials</a> convicted several Nazi leaders of crimes against humanity involving the Holocaust. However, there remained several movements and governments that were ideologically related to fascism.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Francisco_Franco" title="Francisco Franco">Francisco Franco</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Falangist" title="Falangist">Falangist</a> one-party state in Spain was officially neutral during World War II and it survived the collapse of the Axis Powers. Franco's rise to power had been directly assisted by the militaries of Fascist Italy and Nazi Germany during the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">Spanish Civil War</a> and Franco had <a href="/wiki/Blue_Division" title="Blue Division">sent volunteers</a> to fight on the side of Nazi Germany against the Soviet Union during World War II. The first years were characterized by a repression against the anti-fascist ideologies, deep censorship and the suppression of democratic institutions (elected Parliament, <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Constitution_of_1931" title="Spanish Constitution of 1931">Spanish Constitution of 1931</a>, Regional Statutes of Autonomy). After World War II and a period of international isolation, Franco's regime normalized relations with the Western powers during the Cold War, until Franco's death in 1975 and the transformation of Spain into a liberal democracy.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:171px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giorgio_Almirante_crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="243" data-file-width="169" decoding="async" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a5/Giorgio_Almirante_crop.jpg" width="169"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Giorgio_Almirante_crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Almirante" title="Giorgio Almirante">Giorgio Almirante</a>, leader of the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Social_Movement" title="Italian Social Movement">Italian Social Movement</a> from 1969 to 1987</div></div></div>
<p>Historian Robert Paxton observes that one of the main problems in defining fascism is that it was widely mimicked. Paxton says: "In fascism's heyday, in the 1930s, many regimes that were not functionally fascist borrowed elements of fascist decor in order to lend themselves an aura of force, vitality, and mass mobilization." He goes on to observe that <a href="/wiki/Ant%C3%B3nio_de_Oliveira_Salazar" title="António de Oliveira Salazar">Salazar</a> "crushed Portuguese fascism after he had copied some of its techniques of popular mobilization."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaxton19983,_17_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaxton19983,_17-191">[191]</a></sup> Paxton says: "Where Franco subjected Spain's fascist party to his personal control, Salazar abolished outright in July 1934 the nearest thing Portugal had to an authentic fascist movement, Rolão Preto's blue-shirted National Syndicalists. ... Salazar preferred to control his population through such 'organic' institutions traditionally powerful in Portugal as the Church. Salazar's regime was not only non-fascist, but 'voluntarily non-totalitarian,' preferring to let those of its citizens who kept out of politics 'live by habit.<span style="padding-right:.15em;">'</span>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaxton2004150_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaxton2004150-192">[192]</a></sup> However, historians tend to view the <a href="/wiki/Estado_Novo_(Portugal)" title="Estado Novo (Portugal)">Estado Novo</a> as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Para-fascist" title="Para-fascist">para-fascist</a> in nature,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaviesLynch2002[httpsarchiveorgdetailsroutledgecompani00davipage237_237]_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaviesLynch2002[httpsarchiveorgdetailsroutledgecompani00davipage237_237]-193">[193]</a></sup> possessing minimal fascist tendencies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPassmore200276_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPassmore200276-194">[194]</a></sup> Other historians, including <a href="/wiki/Fernando_Rosas" title="Fernando Rosas">Fernando Rosas</a> and Manuel Villaverde Cabral, think that the Estado Novo should be considered fascist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERosas2019_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERosas2019-195">[195]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (October 2021)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In Argentina, <a href="/wiki/Peronism" title="Peronism">Peronism</a>, associated with the regime of <a href="/wiki/Juan_Per%C3%B3n" title="Juan Perón">Juan Perón</a> from 1946 to 1955 and 1973 to 1974, was influenced by fascism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlamires2006512_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlamires2006512-196">[196]</a></sup> Between 1939 and 1941, prior to his rise to power, Perón had developed a deep admiration of Italian Fascism and modelled his economic policies on Italian fascist policies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlamires2006512_196-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlamires2006512-196">[196]</a></sup> However, not all historians agree with this identification,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> which they consider debatable<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> or even false,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> biased by a pejorative political position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> Other authors, such as the Israeli <a href="/wiki/Raanan_Rein" title="Raanan Rein">Raanan Rein</a>, categorically maintain that Perón was not a fascist and that this characterization was imposed on him because of his defiant stance against US hegemony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term neo-fascism refers to fascist movements after World War II. In Italy, the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Social_Movement" title="Italian Social Movement">Italian Social Movement</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Almirante" title="Giorgio Almirante">Giorgio Almirante</a> was a major neo-fascist movement that transformed itself into a self-described "post-fascist" movement called the <a href="/wiki/National_Alliance_(Italy)" title="National Alliance (Italy)">National Alliance</a> (AN), which has been an ally of <a href="/wiki/Silvio_Berlusconi" title="Silvio Berlusconi">Silvio Berlusconi</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Forza_Italia" title="Forza Italia">Forza Italia</a> for a decade. In 2008, AN joined Forza Italia in Berlusconi's new party <a href="/wiki/The_People_of_Freedom" title="The People of Freedom">The People of Freedom</a>, but in 2012 a group of politicians split from The People of Freedom, refounding the party with the name <a href="/wiki/Brothers_of_Italy" title="Brothers of Italy">Brothers of Italy</a>. In Germany, various neo-Nazi movements have been formed and banned in accordance with Germany's constitutional law which forbids Nazism. The <a href="/wiki/National_Democratic_Party_of_Germany" title="National Democratic Party of Germany">National Democratic Party of Germany</a> (NPD) is widely considered a neo-Nazi party, although the party does not publicly identify itself as such.
</p>
<h3><span id="Contemporary_fascism_.282008.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_fascism_(2008–present)">Contemporary fascism (2008–present)</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Alt-right" title="Alt-right">Alt-right</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radical_right_(United_States)" title="Radical right (United States)">Radical right (United States)</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fascism_in_the_United_States" title="Fascism in the United States">Fascism in the United States</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Greece">Greece</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Golden_Dawn_(Greece)" title="Golden Dawn (Greece)">Golden Dawn</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Golden_Dawn_demonstration_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="457" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Golden_Dawn_demonstration_1.jpg/220px-Golden_Dawn_demonstration_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Golden_Dawn_demonstration_1.jpg/330px-Golden_Dawn_demonstration_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Golden_Dawn_demonstration_1.jpg/440px-Golden_Dawn_demonstration_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Golden_Dawn_demonstration_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Golden Dawn demonstration in Greece in 2012</div></div></div>
<p>After the onset of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Recession" title="Great Recession">Great Recession</a> and economic crisis in Greece, a movement known as the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Dawn_(Greece)" title="Golden Dawn (Greece)">Golden Dawn</a>, widely considered a neo-Nazi party, soared in support out of obscurity and won seats in Greece's parliament, espousing a staunch hostility towards minorities, illegal immigrants and refugees. In 2013, after the murder of an anti-fascist musician by a person with links to Golden Dawn, the Greek government ordered the arrest of Golden Dawn's leader <a href="/wiki/Nikolaos_Michaloliakos" title="Nikolaos Michaloliakos">Nikolaos Michaloliakos</a> and other members on charges related to being associated with a criminal organization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup> On 7 October 2020, Athens Appeals Court announced verdicts for 68 defendants, including the party's political leadership. Nikolaos Michaloliakos and six other prominent members and former MPs were found guilty of running a criminal organization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TheConversation_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-TheConversation-204">[204]</a></sup> Guilty verdicts on charges of murder, attempted murder, and violent attacks on immigrants and left-wing political opponents were delivered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-Soviet_Russia">Post-Soviet Russia</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Rashism" title="Rashism">Rashism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Putinism" title="Putinism">Putinism</a></div>
<p>Before and during the <a href="/wiki/2022_Russian_invasion_of_Ukraine" title="2022 Russian invasion of Ukraine">2022 Russian invasion of Ukraine</a>, scholars discussed whether Russia's regime under Putin is fascist. According to <a href="/wiki/Alexander_J._Motyl" title="Alexander J. Motyl">Alexander J. Motyl</a>, an American historian and political scientist, Russian fascism has the following characteristics:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>An <a href="/wiki/Dictatorship" title="Dictatorship">undemocratic political system</a>, different from both traditional authoritarianism and totalitarianism;</li>
<li>Statism and hypernationalism;</li>
<li>A hypermasculine <a href="/wiki/Cult_of_personality" title="Cult of personality">cult of the supreme leader</a> (emphasis on his courage, militancy and physical prowess);</li>
<li>General popular support for the regime and its leader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Yale historian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timothy_Snyder" title="Timothy Snyder">Timothy Snyder</a> has stated that "Putin's regime is [...] the world center of fascism" and has written an article entitled <i>"We Should Say It: Russia Is Fascist."</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laruelle_2022_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-Laruelle_2022-209">[209]</a></sup> Alternately, Oxford historian <a href="/wiki/Roger_Griffin" title="Roger Griffin">Roger Griffin</a> compared Putin's Russia to the World War II-era <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Empire of Japan</a>, saying that like Putin's Russia, it "emulated fascism in many ways, but was not fascist."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rferl-russia_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-rferl-russia-210">[210]</a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/Stanley_G._Payne" title="Stanley G. Payne">Stanley G. Payne</a> says Putin's Russia "is not equivalent to the fascist regimes of World War II, but it forms the nearest analogue to fascism found in a major country since that time" and argues that that Putin's political system is "more a revival of the creed of Tsar <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_I_of_Russia" title="Nicholas I of Russia">Nicholas I</a> in the 19th century that emphasized 'Orthodoxy, autocracy, and nationality' than one resembling the revolutionary, modernizing regimes of Hitler and Mussolini."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rferl-russia_210-1"><a href="#cite_note-rferl-russia-210">[210]</a></sup>
</p><p>Scholars point out that fascism is "a revolutionary form of nationalism" seeking to destroy the old system and remake society, and Putin is a reactionary politician who is not trying to create a new order "but to recreate a modified version of the Soviet Union", according to Griffin. Genuine fascists in Russia, like deceased politician <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Zhirinovsky" title="Vladimir Zhirinovsky">Vladimir Zhirinovsky</a> and activist and self-styled philosopher <a href="/wiki/Aleksandr_Dugin" title="Aleksandr Dugin">Aleksandr Dugin</a>, "describe in their writings a completely new Russia" controlling parts of the world that were never under tsarist or Soviet domination, German scientist <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Umland" title="Andreas Umland">Andreas Umland</a> said.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rferl-russia_210-2"><a href="#cite_note-rferl-russia-210">[210]</a></sup> Marlene Laurelle argues that simply calling contemporary Russia "fascist" is inadequate as it misses a more "complex and differentiated" view.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laruelle_2022_209-1"><a href="#cite_note-Laruelle_2022-209">[209]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Radio_Free_Europe/Radio_Liberty" title="Radio Free Europe/Radio Liberty">Radio Free Europe/Radio Liberty</a>, collecting the opinions of experts on fascism, said that while Russia is repressive and authoritarian, it cannot be classified as a fascist state for various reasons, including Russia's government being more reactionary than revolutionary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tenets">Tenets</span></h2>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_O._Paxton" title="Robert O. Paxton">Robert O. Paxton</a> finds that even though fascism "maintained the existing regime of property and social hierarchy," it cannot be considered "simply a more muscular form of conservatism" because "fascism in power did carry out some changes profound enough to be called 'revolutionary.<span style="padding-right:.15em;">'</span>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaxton200411_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaxton200411-212">[212]</a></sup> These transformations "often set fascists into conflict with conservatives rooted in families, churches, social rank, and property." Paxton argues that "fascism redrew the frontiers between private and public, sharply diminishing what had once been untouchably private. It changed the practice of citizenship from the enjoyment of constitutional rights and duties to participation in mass ceremonies of affirmation and conformity. It reconfigured relations between the individual and the collectivity, so that an individual had no rights outside community interest. It expanded the powers of the executive—party and state—in a bid for total control. Finally, it unleashed aggressive emotions hitherto known in Europe only during war or social revolution."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaxton200411_212-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaxton200411-212">[212]</a></sup>
</p>
